RATING: 8
It's hard not to like this. Eagle have finally gotten a fully developed game to rank alongside their excellent production values. This, of course, is down to Martin Wallace and the fact that the game has in essence undergone a long development cycle right back to Lancashire Railways. I hesitate to call Railroad Tycoon 'Age of Steam' lite as some have done because it isn't really lite. It just places more emphasis of the 'fun' aspects of the game rather than Age of Steam's tendency to push the 'gamer' limits. RT certainly has more theme to it than AoS and is more forgiving to the casual gamer. AoS remains my favourite game but I'm quite happy to own both.

RATING: 10
Age of Steam lite (sort of), which means tile-laying (tracks), route-planning, tough economic decisions (not as tough as AoS), and a strong theme (unlike AoS).

A very thematic, overproduced game about building up a railway empire.  It's definitely a revised version of Age of Steam (AoS), but has noticeable differences in addition to its many similarities.  For starters, the game plays 2-6, which is a big plus, and it is much easier to jump in and play.  In other words, although this is a high strategy game, it is much less gruelling than AoS, which is a good thing for beginners, and even myself who rates AoS very highly.

Gameplay involves getting money (shares to start usually) in order to build track, in order to ship goods, which nets you real money, as well as points.  There is an auction for player order, then you have 3 turns each per round, but of course you want to do much more than just 3 things, as does everyone else.  You can buy track (and the one-link limit makes the game much more accessible, as people can't be immediately locked out of certain regions), you can ship a good (only one per turn, making it again harder to completely drain resources immediately), or select a card (and then possibly use it).  The cards range in ability, but of course you can always bid higher to have first choice.

You also have a tycoon card which is a hidden bonus victory condition for you, if you achieve it.  Taking shares is simple- you just take as many as you need, but you have to pay $1000 each round, and they are minus 1 point per share at the end of the game.  Tough yes, but not as devastating as in AoS.  You also make "real" money sooner in this game, and thus don't feel absolutely broke the whole game, although there is an income reduction that occurs only if you get way up in points.

Overall this is an awesome game if you like AoS, as you actually want to play the game, rather than subject yourself to an exercise in economic decision making torture (re: AoS).  That's not to say there aren't tough decisions in Railroad Tycoon (RRT), it's just that the consequences are less stringent.  I think with more plays RRT will outshine AoS for me, in large part because it will see the table more often and reach a larger audience of players.

Own the [boardgame=30450]Railways of Europe[/boardgame], [boardgame=87627]Railways of Mexico[/boardgame], [boardgame=42964]Railways of England & Wales[/boardgame], [boardgame=72267]Railways of the Western U.S.[/boardgame], [boardgame=72268]Railways Through Time[/boardgame], [boardgame=131386]Railways of North America[/boardgame], and [boardgame=99692]Event deck[/boardgame] expansions, which definitely breath new life into this great game.

RATING: 8
I must admit I like this game more than I thought I would. The gameplay has just the right amount of depth -- enough rules and options to be interesting but not overwhelming. The game length is a workable 2 hours or so (even with 5 players). All 3 games I've played have been very different, which bodes well for replayability (and I haven't even touched Chicago yet!). Negatives: quality problems with the board and components (see sbabcock's review).

RATING: 7.9
After a couple of plays (Railroad Tycoon), I must say that it's an improvement over Age of Steam (AOS). 

Things I like:

* It's faster playing (vs. AOS) with games ending so quickly that it can catch you off guard.The railroad operation cards are a very nice touch adding randomness to the game and lots of tactical goals to take advantage of. More ways to victory versus the AOS system.

* Building a network is more organic and realistic in this game with much, much less of the tricky, spiral track building found in a successful AOS victory. 

* While the economics aren't painful like AOS, there's a subtle importance of being thrifty and wise with expenditures. 

* Auctions are not quite the ugly, thrashing battles of AOS but are generally more dignified with an occasional battle very early in the game over geographic position or over a very important railroad card.

Now things I don't like:

* Okay let me get it out - the game board is RIDICULOUS in its size. It's simply not designed with the game players in mind. It's more like a model railroader decided to create a map and pieces. The map should be 30-40% smaller for true competitive play and tournament play. Important railroad operation cards can be a long distance from 1 or more players. Some people talk about their place at the table actually determining where they will build because it's easier to see what's in front of you and they're lazy. I understand that point. 

* Also, I don't like the way the victory point track was created. It's very difficult to keep the locos where they should be on the track and the printing of VP's and income is just too small. The older I get, the more this last point is important.

* The rules are vague in a couple of important places and a set of revised rules or official FAQ is something truly needed.

* Three player game balance is very tricky. Two players is straight forward and four players is probably the sweet spot. Five and Six player games probably make for a longer more unruly game but that wouldn't effect play balance. It might have been nice if specific scenarios had been created for 2 and 3 player games (beyond initial cube distribution and number of empty city markers). Scenarios that take out geographic portions of the board (along with certain cards) and maybe give players a specific Tycoon card as a goal.

Overall, I like the game as railroad games go. Is it my favorite? Not sure. I really, really like Ticket to Ride/Marklin Edition. But more RRT plays may change my mind as to my favorite... or not.

RATING: 6.5
Hmm...well, I'm no big AoS fan, so you have to take that into account here.  This has 80% of what AoS has.  So, it is not really "AoS-lite" - more AoS midi.

The faults that AoS has, for me, are mainly repeated here.  This comes down to the facts that it doesn't score well for me as either a brain burner or a as fun game.  As a  brain-burner it gets too repetitive and mathematical; in fun terms, it gets kind of dull at the end - rinse and repeat...just ship a load of stuff for as much as you can.  Ok, so the other train game that's mega popular here (TtR) does the same (keep drawing cards), but somehow that game is more fun to play than this...probably because until the end you always have something you can do, like draw new routes or end the game early, or score 15 pointers. Granted TtR is about 40% as deep as this (depends on the version), but it's about 500% more fun.  Here, the last third kind of plays itself - you've made your bed (track is laid) and you just endlessly ship stuff to make it (your point score) as cosy as you can.

Yet, I feel that this is marginally better than AoS.  I don't like the huge board (I'd shave 20% off of it) which makes me feel that I need to stand and move around to get perspective, or the silly, badly designed scoring track and I don't think that the cards add that much other than a bit of extra confusion.  On the other hand, the share system is better here and I also slightly prefer the handling of the turns and since the bidding doesn't seem to matter much I think that helps the game balance a little (even if the bidding can be dull as a result).

Overall, I think "the joys" of the AoS-type system have mainly escaped me.  I remain distinctly uninspired by either.

RATING: 7.5
Update: 2009 10 07: I downgraded this a bit. Now that Steam is out, I have a game with the parts I like about RRT without the parts I found questionable. RRT is still a very fun game, but the length, size and wonkiness (particularly with USA board) makes it somewhat less attractive to play.

Mechanics are simple without being simplistic. This is a great game to use when trying to lead someone beyond "gateway" games such as Ticket to Ride or Carcassonne because it's quite straighforward yet there is depth there.

RATING: 9
This is my favorite train game overall. yes I really love ticket to ride but this game takes that and adds more options to your play.  Not only do you have to figure out your routes but you have to ship the goods to make money. Also don't forget to upgrade your trains, pay attention to how much debt your collecting, get special cards to give you more overall abilities AND you can bid on your play order. I really enjoy the step up from the beginner level games like TTR.

RATING: 1.1
There are soooo many cool things you could do with a train game.  Think about it: you could have dinosaurs drive those trains.  You could have lasers mounted on your trains.  You could have Johnny Cash in it.  Any of which would be so cool, you couldn't HELP but to get a 10 rating out of it.

So what do we do?  We create a crappy Euro with your typical moronic Euro-theme.  My city turns out, let's say, TVs.  My TVs are popular, so I sell out of all of them.  What do I do?  I borrow money so that I can expand my production and make MORE TVs, right?  No.  I STOP SELLING THEM!  I ran out of cubes.  I can't make any more.  How much pot does one have to smoke in order to believe this?  Seriously?  

Look, I can work with some things: let's say the dinosaurs on my train hopped off and rampaged through your city, eating everyone in sight and using their laser guns to destroy every TV.  Let's say Johnny Cash got tired of hearing that whistle blowing, so he led a prison breakout at Folsom and they looted your city and stole your TVs.  Fine.  THAT, I can work with (and admit it: both options would be so awesome).  

But no, we don't do that.  We move our cubes from city to city and collect monopoly money and pay off our mortgages, or some type of Euro-crappy-nonsense.  And worst of all, unlike your typical 45-minute Ticket to Ride-type of Euro that has nothing to do with anything, you are on the hook for a few hours with this turd.

So much you could do with a train theme. :shake:

FAIL.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[i]2017/02/03 1 play 4p[/i]

Surprisingly, this is a very approachable game, that could have been a gateway game, save for the play length.

Production values of the components are great, with a beautiful board and bits.

The game is tense, thanks to bidding and the racing to lay track, reach towns, deny opponents and snag goal cards.

The Eastern US board seems unbalanced, but I guess this is naturally corrected by player experience. I would need more plays to know for certain.

The variable income track also helps in shaping a satisfying game progression arc.

One of the best entry level train/pick-up and delivery games.

RATING: 8
Very good, very clean track building and resource delivering game. I do wish there was a little bit more you could do with your money or debt to make things more interesting, but the special action cards add at least a little bit extra decision content to the game state.

I like that the design is intuitive and have enjoyed every play so far. But part of that is that I've had access to a wide variety of maps to play with that change the feel of every game. The system itself can feel a bit samey a bit too quickly in my opinion without the availability of more maps.

RATING: 10
It's one of my favorite games. Both on the computer and the board game. It's got great game mechanics. Easy to learn. Few extras that doesn't really make someone win or loose. I've got the first release, and the board got a few flaws. The blue is too similar to black and purple, and it bends/warps so you get huge gaps. This doesn't ruin the gaming experience at all.

Tip: you need a large table to play on. An average kitchen table is not wide enough. It's bigger than a game of Twilight Empirium.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I can really only compare this to Steam, but I can safely say that I really like both games.  Each game has its own merits and even though I already own Steam, I can see myself picking this up in the future.

What makes these games so great for me is that the choices presented are all well integrated with the theme, they give players a large variety of options each turn and they challenge players but not in a way I find too brain burning.

Gameplay for railways is nice and simple (with a helpful card/ player aid to remind players of options each turn).  The rail baron cards and ease of laying track makes this game an easy step up from games such as Ticket to Ride, while still providing some familiar mechanics and goals.  The Railroad Operation cards allow for some spontaneity to keep both the game and player tactics fresh.  I especially like how various card powers can be combo'd (Perfect Engineering + Government Grant).  But as much as I like the cards, they also create some really lucky situations for various players whose tracks and strategies line up with card requirements better.  I also don't like how goods replaced are random.  The Western link rules are also a bit of a bother to explain, especially with new gamers.  With that being said however, I still find the game very engaging and very fun.

The components in Railways are almost perfect.  I have issues with the track tiles being the same type of track on both sides and with the lack of tight turn track tiles (which would have been useful in a number of instances).  The players' train components are all great, but some of the empty city makers are a bit oversized.  Additionally the card graphics indicating bonus points for various links are really unclear (as in the resolution on the cards are too low that some values are unreadable).

Overall, Railways of the World is a great game, one which I find a little more lucky than Steam, but a whole lot more accessible especially to newer gamers.  I hope to be able to acquire a copy of this soon.

RATING: 6
A "lighter" version of Age of Steam with nice components and chrome.  Play is lighter for 2 primary reasons - money planning and goods.  In Railroad Tycoon, stocks can be taken as money is needed, thus reducing the planning required.  All goods are placed on the board prior to start of game.  This gives  the game a bit of predetermined-ness.  The new element in Tycoon are cards.  The Tycoon card helps new player form initial plans but is not really needed for more experienced players.  Other cards introduce an element of randomness; especially when area specific cards are turned up that are only truly viable for 1 or 2 players.  Overall the game has a nice glossy feel, but lacks the tighter and cleaner design of Age of Steam.  [GTI Code: TPCD]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Feb-29-2008
Pros:
1. I love the train markers!
2. I like that the track tiles are much smaller than those in AoS.
3. All the cubes are on the board.
4. I like that you can build, deliver, buy train in any order and number that you want.

Cons:
1. Board too big because you don't use a whole third of the board.
2. For such a big board the NE is always full of people and is like a sixth of the whole board.
3. The cities are so far apart that there is so much dead space around the tracks connecting cities.  Also since cities are so far from each other and the board so huge, it is very hard to try to stop the leader because it will cost you the game if you try to build tracks to where he/she is.
4. The auction for turn order I find to be a huge flaw.  2nd place gets a silver plate for free and the last gets shafted because the player to his left wants to be first all the time.
5. One has to know all the possible major lines in the deck to do well.  Also the fact that you can complete major lines before it is available provides a lot of confusion on who gets it etc.  The biggest problem I have with major lines is that it rewards people for doing something they should be doing anyways.  Major lines gives you points for connecting various cities with your tracks.  Building that infrastructure to set you up for deliveries is enough reward/incentive for you to do.  Getting points/income for the sake of building is just over the top.  
6. The rules for building over river is tedious.  One must always check if there is a river/mountain under a track tile to see if the next player that wants to build in the same hex has to pay for the extra costs.
7. With less than 6 players the game is too open and does not provide enough tension or urgency to do things before anyone else.
8. Unless every player knows the value of the NE then the game will be skewed.  Either not enough competition in the NE that gives those guys a benefit or too much competition that they end up shooting each other on the foot.
9. Some Tycoon cards are just inherently better than others and easier to meet.

Wow. I knew that I didn't want to play the game anymore, but actually listing the Pros and Cons really help you build your resolve.

RATING: 9
[i]Posted: 4/8/07, Rated: 9[/i]

The verdict is in: I love train games.  No, that's not an invitation to exhume the worn-out debate over the definition of a train game.  Trains have universal appeal.  I like the theme.  My family enjoys it, and my friends enjoy it.  That's good enough for me.

[i]Railroad Tycoon[/i] was the next logical step after [i][thing=9209]Ticket to Ride[/thing][/i] and [i][thing=94]Union Pacific[/thing][/i].  [i]Railroad Tycoon[/i] provides additional layers of complexity by allowing players to lay out their tracks, instead of providing pre-printed segments.  Set collection isn't a part of [i]Railroad Tycoon.[/i]  Segments, or "links" as they're called, are not exclusive: it is possible for other players to use your rails, to your benefit.

Efficiently building your infrastructure, managing your debt, and delivering the desired goods to each city is the core of this game.  Each one of those items sounds like it is complicated, but Wallace pulls it all off with aplomb.  The rules feel very streamlined, and are easy to understand.  Once a few example turns have been provided, most new players are ready to jump in with both feet.

Production values abound, almost to the point of being ostentatious.  The box is [i]huge,[/i] and heavy with components.  Players use various hex counters to lay out their tracks.  Obviously that is not a problem for veteran gamers, but the eyes of new gamers can gloss over when faced with their first hex map.  The map itself is gargantuan, having three, tri-fold sections which are laid together on the table.  I think that I have a fairly large dining room table, and the full game board does fit, although there isn't a lot of extra room along the sides.  Side note: four small (3/4") binder clips work very well to hold the board together during play.

[i]Railroad Tycoon[/i] is a superbly produced, engaging strategy game with streamlined rules, and a romantic theme.  It does appeal, but its sheer size can be intimidating.  I admit to loving train games.  Despite my enthusiasm, [i]Railroad Tycoon[/i] hasn't been as well received as [i][thing=9209]Ticket to Ride[/thing][/i] or [i][thing=94]Union Pacific[/thing][/i].  A game that receives a "10" should be perfection, so [i]Railroad Tycoon[/i] earns a well-deserved and respectable "9."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

The only minor downside is that you need a good large playing area, preferably a big table or two combined, but once you have that set up it never fails to stay fun.

RATING: 6
This game would have been an eight or a nine if not for its many problems.

Things that suck about Railroad Tycoon:
- Its turn order auction system is terrible.
- Most of the meaningful analysis that's done during the game is done right after the cubes are placed on the board.  The rest of the game plays itself.
- It has a horrendous amount of setup time.  When compared to this game, Puerto Rico looks like Othello.
- Most of the cards you see during the game are seen right at the beginning, compounding the second problem.
- The roller coaster income track was a nice idea, but was poorly implemented.  In most games, you don't earn enough VPs to see your income plummet, and even when you do, you're unlikely to have monetary problems by the end of the game.
- With a typical cube distribution, if one person starts in the northeast, he's in great shape, but if two people start there, they're both screwed.

RATING: 8.5
Update (Sep 11): Two more expansion maps are now available for RRT/RotW - Western USA (for up to six players)and Mexico (2-3 players) along with a series-wide event deck and some new elements ("rotor" cities and fuel depots). These have all been positive additions and I am becoming more appreciative of the game for its accessibility and just plain fun factor. More random elements than its cousins surely, but I find I'm enjoying it more than I did before. Based on the fixes and re-design that went into "Railways of the World," my rating has gone back up. I still believe that "Steam" is a better "gamer's game" but I'm happy to play RRT/RotW anytime.

Update (Sep 09): With the arrival of "Steam: Rails to Riches" from Martin Wallace out of Mayfair Games, I have downgraded RRT. "Steam" is now the game you want if AoS is a bit too harsh for you. However, if you are partial to the "Rails of the World" series (first exp was "Rails of Europe," next was "Railways of England and Wales"), you may wish to keep RRT to be able to play those (or hope they can be modified to use the "Steam" system).

The title might suggest a reference to Sid Meier's classic PC game with a similar title. In my view, the game is really inspired by Martin Wallace's "Age of Steam" and was co-designed by him (no report of Sid having anything to do with it). It is the finest game that Eagle Games has yet produced in its brief existence. It is on one level very like AoS until you get into the finer details.

Gone are the roles and the nail-biting financial tight-rope (here, you can issue more shares anytime you need extra cash). Added are bonus VP cards and Tycoon cards that assist in pushing you along besides simply delivering goods cubes to similar colored cities. The track laying is very similar, and urbanization, upgrading choo-choos and adding extra goods cubes are all there but implemented in different ways.

The map board is huge! You better check the dimensions before you commit to playing on your dining room table. You get a big box here and it is stuffed (altho with a lot of plastic that plays no real role in the game other than to provide three-dimensional color).

A much more accessible game than AoS and quite a bit more random, yet retaining a lot that made AoS fun while adding some additional fun elements of its own. I have both games and have no hesitation playing either.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Cleaned up version of Railroad Tycoon. Some of the "tweaking" that went into the first two expansions have been reverse engineered back into the base game and it is the better for it. Some of the operation cards have been fixed and the method for choosing the Baron cards improved.

The map has been improved graphically and adjusted slightly around New Orleans, again, for the better. It has also been made smaller by the removal of the scoring track from around the outside (and removing some superfluous graphics). But, you now need the separate scoring track as an additional board to set up beside the map, so you still need a lot of real estate to play this game.

Because of the mysterious way ratings are derived here, this game rates below its progenitor despite being distinctly better. I hope that will eventually age out. This game should now be at least in the top thirty on the Geek meter.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Railways was introduced to me a couple of years ago and it has taken up a regular place among the games we play very frequently. It can be a bit long and have some analysis paralysis problems, but the various expansions with smaller boards help that issue. Plus this reprinted version comes with the Mexico map, which is great for two players. 

It even holds up well for tournament and competitive play, as I've tried that at GenCon the past two years. About the only downside is that I believe with the major lines on the Eastern U.S. map, there are only two truly valid strategies for winning a 5-6 player game. With such an open world design, it should be easier to compete without having to play the same way every time. But that's just one map and really is not a concern on other maps and expansions. We love this game series and will continue to play it for years. 

However, just because some guys who played the map 100 times have solved it under a particular rules set does not mean the game is not worth your time. On the contrary, you will likely find hours and hours of enjoyment here. This is my favorite "heavy" board game to show to people, as it is simple enough to grasp but difficult to master. Kind of like chess in that way.

RATING: 8.5
1 4p game, 1 5p game.  Loved it!  Lots of decisions, and definitely has depth that grows on you - as you learn the game, you start by playing against the game (how can I best optimize what I have).  Later, you play against the other players (how can I use what I think they'll do to get me a few more points).  Good blend of strategy and tactics, and accessible to non-gamers as long as they are patient non-gamers.  We found proper play made the game longer (as we got close to the end, everyone not in the lead tried to replenish cities to give themselves a chance to keep up), which I think is a weakness.  I would like a variant with a better end game condition.

RATING: 9.5
  Excellent game that will continue to produce variety in a players strategy, game after game, due to the 'Tycoon' card mission and the random placement of commodities across the board during set-up (all players can survey the board for ideas before the game begins).
  For a group of 20-something game geeks slamming pepsi's and hershey bars at the local RPG store 'til midnight this game will be too light.  But, this is a nod to its strength.  The rules are easy enough to get a family playing in ten minutes and complex enough to pull them into the world of gaming.  Victory Points and the multiphase turn are staples of medium-heavy games and the exposure of it in this game is clean and easily understood. 
  While you could call it a good gateway game it's more important to see it as a well developed game that generates a good tension and is a lot of fun.  There is always more to do than you have moves to do it. And boy, do I like trains!!

RATING: 7
I really like Railroad Tycoon.  It took everything I really liked from Age of Steam (pick up & deliver, track building) and then made it so you could issue shares at any time rather than trying to figure out and budget what you need ahead of time only to find it all exploding in your face.  The variable event cards are thrown in and the freedom of choices are made much more wide open and voila you have an excellent railroad game that sits in complexity halfway between Union Pacific/Ticket to Ride and EuroRails/Empire Builder and the other crayon rail games.  After my second play the rating stays the same but I have to make the following comments:  the game plays better with 6 than with 4 (5 would probably be better than 4 as well, and 3 not so good) as there needs to be more competition in the game.  Also, the game feels like it ends too soon.  Last of all, I wish there were more cards in the game that come out.  The cards and the competition drive the auction for turn order and without enough cards and without enough competition why bid?  Better yet, just play with 5 or 6.  Last of all, the $30,000 western links seem like a bad investment.  I haven't tried it yet so I could be wrong.

RATING: 8

RATING: 6
Rating after only one play with four players. I felt like I had quite a bit of control over the income aspect of the game, and I was able to stay solvent (and become the richest player) by only issuing one share during the whole game. But I didn't feel like there was much control over the building and transport aspects of the game, and the victory point system (mostly based on cumulative deliveries) didn't make much thematic sense to me. After all, wasn't this a business? Weren't the railroad tycoons mainly concerned with making money? The snatching up of commodities from under player's noses seemed appropriate given the setting, but the limited availability of routes did not. Why can there only be four tracks into Chicago? And only one into Dover? The game might be improved by allowing players to purchase victory points at the end of the game with cash on hand, based on the turn end dividends printed by the points. Thematically, this would be like selling the line and retiring. Also, sorting through the tiles to find the right sections of track slowed the game down more than it should have. This is an area where the crayon rail games have a clear advantage over the tile rail games. Anyway, Railroad Tycoon is not a game I would play often, but it's one I'm willing to play again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
UPDATE: With Steam: Rails to Riches now out from Mayfair, this game is not just mediocre but also obsolete. Steam finally gets this, if not right, at least pretty close to right. Time may show that the Mayfair version of this system has flaws too, but at least they aren't glaringly obvious.
---
Hmm. Does not appear to be seriously developed or playtested. I enjoy some of the improvements over Age of Steam, and it plays more quickly and more cleanly, and without the gratuitous difficulty of its predecessor. But, it's got a truly horrible runaway leader problem - last time we played, the standings did not change for the last two-thirds of the game. You have to have a strong start to win, and by strong start, I mean the first 2-3 turns. You can be completely screwed with no hope of winning before the halfway mark. The game really, really wants to be at least an hour shorter. And the various cards seem crazy unbalanced and the way they are brought out (a ton up front, then a trickle as the game goes on) seems a little nuts. And the various Tycoon cards are insanely random and unbalanced, although they are admittedly not likely to swing the game except amongst experienced players - but I doubt the game is really good enough that many players are going to get to that point.

So anyway, like everything from Eagle or Warfrog, it feels like it's at best about half baked. It's closer to being done than many of their games, which is not saying very much.

Rienk
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[07/11/05]Railroad Tycoon and Antike were great successes at our recent post-Essen gaming weekend. We're mostly Age of Steams veterans and very much enjoyed this reengineered version of Matin Wallace's masterpiece.

PROS

- HISTORY AND GEOGRAPHY

RT puts you in the shoes of the legendary "robber barons" and portrays the growth of the railway industry far more convingly than AoS seeks and manages to do. There is a true geographic dimension. The city density on the Eastern seaboard, the craggy Appalachians, the plentiful Mid-West etcetc. Of course, the far greater number of hexes and the 4 tile building limit enabled this all.

The railway operation cards and the Tycoon cards add further flavour. With RT you can relive American railway history much as with 1830. AoS - partly because of the standard map being confined to the Mid-West, partly because of its rules - never managed to offer that same experience I crave for.

- GRAPHICS

While I do find Eagle Games' graphics usually way too bombastic and poor taste, RT fortunately does not belong to that category. It's a pleasure to behold. It's almost as if you're building a miniature railway model!

- ECONOMIC SYSTEM

I personally like the income bell curve. A better solution than the taxes found in AoS. Also, it really makes a "few shares" strategy truly viable. Perhaps too much so. While in my very first game of RT I issued - following typical AoS-type expansive play - a whopping amount of 13 shares (and almost won in doing so), I haven't seen any similar strategy since. A pity. We're getting too disciplined for my taste. I very much hope that in the long run both financially displined and agressive strategies prove equally viable.

I dare say this ties in with RT lacking one of my favourite AoS elements: the fledgling railway companies need time and further capital to reach the break-even". Very nice simulation touch. I wish RT would have a tougher advanced economic system for those veterans enjoying this particular aspect of AoS. I dare say it won't be before long, before I start tinkering with various variants to this end...

- CUBE DISTRIBUTION AND ENDGAME CONDITION

The pre-game distribution of all goods save for those gained by urbanization or city growth is a considerable improvement over AoS. Reduces an unnecessary element of luck.

The endgame condition resulting from this change happens to be a stroke of genius. The variability actually allows for new player tactics. Well done.

- LESS AOS GAMEYNESS:

At times AoS was very gamey indeed, which worked against the "railway simulation" character of the game. While not going into the specifics, I do consider this less of a problem with RT.

CONS:

- The auction isn't nearly as interesting. The first and some of the following ones are interesting (particularly when valuable operation cards pop up), but I personally fail to see why much of the fun was taken out of the auctions. I like the fact that the old privileges have been turned into regular actions, but the introduction of the operations cards could have presented a sufficient substitute.

I'm presently considering a possible variant in which say 3-6 operation cards are added to the display each turn (depending on player number). Then the turn order is auctioned off in AoS style and players then can pick one card for free (save obviously those green ones).
Well, lots of variations along similar lines to consider...

The beauty of making the auction more important is of course that money becomes correspondingly tighter. After all, the more you spend on the auctions, the more shares will you need and the higher your liabilities will probably  be...

What else to I dislike or miss? Hm, I would have added a town or two here and there. For instance, one minor town in between Chootanooga (or however that place is spelt) and Virginia . Historically a railroad was built along that Appallachian valley. I don't really  see one built there in RT. A shame really. The game ought to make such historical routes viable.

As for the Western link, well, we haven't seen anyone use it in our three games this last weekend. I hope the option is attractive enough. Clearly a late game option. I just hope people will find it at times useful enough. Otherwise we might lower the price...

BOTTOM LINE:

I believe I prefer Railroad Tycoon. It is true: I miss the break even point, tighter finances and more interesting auctions from AoS, but the better simulation qualities win the day for me.

RATING: 6.6
This game is Age of Steam lite.  It took the best parts of Age of Steam to make a faster game, which is good because the game is so tactical.  I'm still not really a fan of either one.  I think whoever gets the least competition will win, and competition with someone can have an adverse affect on your position.  So you either let them win, or take them out of it with yourself and let someone else win.  The blocking is significant, and if somebody decides to battle you hard, you might as well give up.  I made the analogy using a beautiful mild tactical game with little screwage, Through the Desert.  I said that this type of game is like Through the Desert, except you get to place 8 camels instead of 2 on your turn.  Do you see the problems with that?  At least it is only 90 minutes and the board is beautiful, although freaking hard to play.  That is why this gets half a point higher than Age of Steam.

RATING: 7
I am not a big fan of train games like Steam. I don’t really know why as I, as a eurogamer, should like everything about them, but they just aren’t that interesting to me. I don’t refuse to play them, but I also wouldn’t actively seek them out and suggest them. This is the case with Steam, and it turned out to be the same with RotW. I’m still undecided about which game I like better as they have different strengths and weaknesses, but RotW is definitely the easier one and probably a bit more forgiving. I liked the different bonus cards and the route bonuses for connecting certain cities as you had some goals to aim for along with your secret objective card. You always have to balance this against moving the cubes in your network and get a decent income, which in this game equals your VP (sort of), which made it easier to understand. I definitely see why some people love these train games, but I prefer other eurogames. I would definitely like to try Age of Steam and Railroad Tycoon, but I cannot see myself playing these games all the time.

Overall, an excellent game.  Make sure you have a very large table to play this on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I don't want to be overly harsh to this game because it is based on the Early Railways system and I do think that is has some innovative new ideas but I personally would not want to play it over any of the Early Railways Series and 99% of the Age of Steam maps out there, even with 6 at the table, so my rating has to reflect that...

RRT is ostensibly the slightly less gamey, more accessible reworking of AoS (note numerous articles available here on the Geek that detail the differences). Certain aspects of it are actually *heavier* and more appealing to hardcore gamers (i.e.: all the goods start on the board) but on the balance the economic system is much more forgiving than AoS and the board is absolutely monstrous in comparison.


Things I liked:

- Operations cards: add flavour to the game and give sub-objectives to shoot for.

- Goods all start on the board. The significant random element of AoS is fixed and this provides a new, interesting end game condition.


Things I dislike:

- Operations cards add a significant luck factor to the game. If the route you are already building has its ops cards come up at the right time then you are basically receiving gifted points, which feels wrong in a game with this amount of forward planning.

- The economic system feels like it has some issues. In one sense it is too easy to get into the black in the early game because the engine no longer counts against you in book keeping. In another the late game seems wrong because AoS's income reduction has been replaced by a an outright declining income mechanism (you peak at about 50 points with 25$ a turn in income and then start going down, so by the end of the game you're actually poor which feels completely wrong) which I think might be easier to deal with, but ends up being much less enjoyable.

- Flaccid Auction. Sometimes good cards come up, which makes for a couple of bids. Most of the time the lack of roles and the lack of tight competition means it's a waste of money. This is a huge negative because interesting auctions not only provide more entertainment for me, but they also suck more money out of the game and create higher debt piles.

- The bits: the goods cubes are huge, as are the player markers, as are town covering empty markers...all of which impede the actual gameplay, particularly when paired with the teeny tiles.

- The board: it is beautiful, and it's big, but it's WAY too big for anything but a full size (6 player) game. The board physically is at least 3 times the size of a standard AoS board, and the hexes on that board only perhaps 3/4 the size of the AoS ones which means there is an absolute tonne of space. Some competition is certainly spurred in the east coast by the number of cities present, but there is such a surfeit of stuff on the board at all times that it never felt like there was an actual lack, which is one of the big crushes in AoS. There also were very few situations where you could really put the thumbscrews to someone with a ship, because there were just so many options available.

- The game plays just as long as AoS does, but sacrifices depth along the way. This is a problem for me...I was hoping that given the streamlining that the game would play significantly faster than AoS, but it didn't seem to be the case. I also think that on the balance all the rules changes do make the game easier to pick up and play, but take just as long to explain as AoS's system which means that it *is* easier for newbies to compete in, but *isn't* easier to get them started on...and if I'm going to take the time to teach a new player a train game I would just prefer to cut straight to AoS. Yes, they'll face a harsher learning curve, but the overall payoff (IMO of course) is better at the end of the day.

On the balance: This game is much prettier than AoS, and has more historical flavour, but it is not as deep, is not significantly shorter, if at all, and has a much larger space requirement. If you're a more hardcore gamer I would recommend sticking to AoS. If you want a lighter, more thematic AoS style game with less cut-throat competition then this would be worth looking into with the caveat that due to rules length this will still require "gamers" as opposed to the casual set, as there is still way too much to digest for non-gamers here.

Note that the rating on this has dropped a further point since I played the Rails of Europe expansion, which takes the idea behind this sytem and makes it into a better game. RoE still has some of the things that annoy me about the base game, but it does eliminate a few of them (i.e.: the point swinging route cards are now just on the board and available, the money track is a little tighter, the board is smaller) which makes the game significantly more enjoyable.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I think I like it better than AoS all things considered, which is really quite shocking since I love AoS.  Railroad Tycoon takes the best parts of AoS and expands them while reducing some of the artificial, abstract game mechanics present that honestly I could live without anyway.  Gone are the privileges, gone is the high degree of competition for roles, and gone is spending most of the game bankrupt.  The result is a game that plays smoother (if not tighter) and is ultimately just more fun to play.  The cards actually work pretty well, giving players short-term goals to work toward and I think the larger board makes for a much more "epic" feel than AoS allowed.  I'm sure the beards-and-bellies crowd will decry this as "dumbed down" AoS, but it just isn't- it's still a competitive, challenging game and adding more theme and more accessibility hasn't hurt the core system at all.  I think the production is excellent, but I still despise the gaudy, baroque artwork Eagle is so keen on and although the three-piece board is impressive it warps like crazy.  All in all, I think it's one of the better games of the year but that's mainly because it's built on one of the better games of the past 10 years.  Ultimately, the game succeeds because it's a hardcore strategy game with enough well-placed accessibility to attract players who might otherwise balk at the stodgy, gamey play of AoS.

RATING: 10
One of my favorite train games, [u]very enjoyable[/u] and very thematic! The only game that makes me feel I build a real railroad network! Wonderful components!
It supports many house rules, so it can be played in different ways.
[i]I play it using my own version of the 2 player "middle board game" variant who makes it the best train game for 2 players:
- use only the middle board;
- remove all the cards, no objectives, no Western Link;
- use Minneapolis & Pittsburgh and all the incomplete northern cities but don't use the incomplete southern cities;
- use the score.xls file from BGG to keeping the score;
- no auction, change the players order each new turn;
- costs: open_hex=2k, river=3k, hill=4k - no other rules, no cost for ridges;
- urbanization cost is 5k. Use the two Western Link tiles for the RED (urbanization) color;
- end the game = 12 empty city markers.[/i]
For a smaller number of players I recommend using the [i]Rails of Europe[/i] expansion (with it's cards) for better variety, balance and scalability.
Mayday sleeved.

RATING: 7
Very nice. After only play, it was hard to get a good feel for the strategy, despite playing Age of Steam many times. I like that it felt fresh.
Things I like more than Age of Steam:
1) Just choose something to do on your turn. Really keeps the game flowing.
2) Take shares whenever you want. Eliminates a really slow period of AoS, and a lot of required number crunching to be sure you're taking as much (and only as much) as you need.
3) Bigger map
4) Cards to keep games from becoming exactly the same, without being totally chaotic.
5) Scoring track with smoother diminishing returns, and no 1-for-1 income from shipped goods, which can create a huge early lead.
Things I don't like as much:
1) I probably won't buy it if only for the obnoxiously large board.
2) Unlikely to have the variety of expansion maps than AoS has.
3) You knid of have to know the cards ahead of time to have a decent strategy. You have to be able to predict certain cards coming out (or at least benefit by doing so), and be able to guess other players' goal cards.
If I didn't already have AoS, I would probably buy RRT, if only because of the "less serious" feel.

RATING: 6
[i]Thanks to BGG's braindead versioning system the original Railroad Tycoon game this comment was written for has been superseded by its revised offspring Railways of the World. Even though changes are reportedly not very major, you are advised to keep this in mind when you read this comment: it applies to RT, not to RotW.[/i]

Ah... Railroad Tycoon. Now there's a conundrum. At my local game group, a couple is absolutely addicted to it, and nearly always bring it along. They tend to play with a fixed group (as the game is long-ish), but I was able to sneak in a go recently. I've played Age of Steam a few times, although it has been too long for me to remember the precise details. Learning Tycoon is fairly easy then, although you still need to pick up the crucial details.

I'm really in two minds about this game. First of all, it plays fairly easily and loosely. There is no sense of the game actively disliking you as is the case with Age of Steam. Having too much shares too early is not a good idea as you can easily do without them after the game is 20 - 25% done, yet they still continue to put a drain on your resources. It makes sense to start more slowly to avoid them altogether. Not so in Age of Steam, where there is no avoiding these pesky buggers. Second is that the board is big and roomy. Too big and roomy, in my humble opinion. It is hard for players to actually interfere with one another because there is nearly always a way out of a sticky situation. And by the time you actually need tiles with crossing railroads, people have such large amounts of money that there is no barrier to them building these tiles whatsoever. Try that with Age of Steam, where costs can be prohibitive to the point where the issuing of a new share is warranted, not to mention the far greater screwage factor of blocking someone's railroad before he can complete the link. In my game, the winners stayed predominantly in the east, making brief excursions over the mountains only when they got a profitable card to do so. Finally, there is a significant advantage to starting in the east because all the cities are close together. That means you are up and running fairly quickly without the need for shares. If you start in the west, you have to issue shares in order to finance a few bits of track.

I seriously wonder about the cards. They give the game a more lively and unpredicatble feel, but at the same time ruin any forward planning. A good card turns up just when someone on the other side of the board can use it? Hello winning for him, hello losing for you. It's simply free transportation of goods, and giving you a good reward for being in the lead already. I was doing fairly well with just building track until the very end, when everyone except me got hold of a good bonus card [i]and[/i] managed to fulfill their secret goal. This leaves me with a slightly sour aftertaste: all that playing and the game hinges on cards drawn at random...??

Make no mistake, I liked playing Railroad Tycoon despite its simplifications. It's fun and engaging, easy to get into, and looks very nice. But I seriously doubt whether the lack of clear constraints as found in Age of Steam (although they might be a bit too constraining :-)) would make this a long-term hit with me—and that's the conundrum, because I secretly do want to play again. 

[b]Update[/b] The expansion Rails of Europe removes many little gripes I have with the base game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Railroad Tycoon (Eagle Games) and now the release Railways of the World is a fantastic railroad shipping game.  The originality lies in the hexagonal design of the map and the random distribution of goods cubes for shipping each game.  I love playing the original game with 5 or 6 players.  The European map (Railways of Europe) is the best map and is great with 3, 4 or 5.  Five players is a tight game on the European map!  The game system is wonderful as players start anywhere on the map they desire.  It is truly open ended competition.  Each player builds their lines from scratch and the cubes deplete over time as shipments are made to various cities of the same color as the cubes.  Additionally, to ship farther and farther, players must spend money to upgrade their locomotives.  The game becomes quite contentious as each player balances potentially borrowing to build out their rail network faster, upgrade their locomotive, and do it in such a way as to "beat other players to the punch".  This game is tremendous fun.  The artwork is amazing as are the game pieces.  The cards add just enough spice to the game and everyone draws a secret objective card at the beginning which sits as a long term goal.  I also own other expansions which are quite fun as well - Railways Through Time, the Western US expansion and Railways of England.  I love this game and most players I know that play this game tend to nominate it for play on a regular occassion - even years later since its debut!!!  Railroad Tycoon/Railways of the World and the various expansions for these great games have made my top 20 list of euro style games of all time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Really fun. Quite a learning curve but excited to get another play under my belt. Could easily be a 10 game. Waiting for my second game!

RATING: N/A
$50 BGG
Revisit the early days of the Age of Steam as you begin with a locomotive (the venerable John Bull, the first locomotive to run in North America) and a vision (your Tycoon "mission" card). From there, build your budding railroad network into a vast empire. Connect New York to Chicago, earn the most money, develop bigger and faster locomotives and maybe even span North America and build the Transcontinental Railway!
Multiple expansions featuring different maps are available. Railways of the World is the new base game for the system and includes the engine placards, railroad tiles, train tokens, money, bonds, and other items that are needed in almost all the Railways of the World series. A gameboard depicting the eastern half of the United States is included in the base game, as well as a mounted map of Mexico.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I thought a long time about what to actually rate this as.  I think the fun could vary quite a bit depending on which of the cards come out - there is a huge luck/how well do you know which routes are on the cards factor.  I've heard people complain about this in T2R but it doesn't bother me there, as T2R is a 45 minute game.  In a 2-3 hr game, having a runaway leader because his chosen starting position happened to have several major line cards come up really is frustrating.

That said, the good things about the game are that money is more forgiving than Age of Steam's system, if that bothers you - and since you don't have to compete for actions, you have more flexibility and autonomy - but this can cause a multi-player solitaire effect in the early game with fewer players.

From a pure usability standpoint, the gameboard is too big and the text on the cards too small.  If you've got a bad back, don't play this one because you'll be sitting down and standing up the whole freaking time.

All in all, for me, I prefer Age of Steam as it doesn't have the luck components.  If you don't mind a large amount of luck in your longer games, or want something less brainburning, you might like this one better.

RATING: 6
"Welcome to AoS lite..."

Ok, should one compare RT with AoS when trying to evaluate it? They share author, theme, and some mechanics. Yet, they have very different targets.

RT is lavishly overproduced, with a (too much!) oversized  board, and marvelous game components, whereas AoS is rather spartan (though functional) production.
All the gamey tricks of AoS have been axed out, replaced by the random cards (spicing everything up): no cutthroath economy, abundance of resources (both money and place on the board), no special roles, a naive (and somehow unfair) clockwise auction for the right of going first.

Clearly, a possible bridge to heavier middleweight games, designed to attract people to economic/route building /pick up and deliver games.

I'd rather play AoS, because the overall playtime is similar, and too much depth has been sacrified to make it ungamer friendly.

I would however gladly play RT, anytime, with 5 or better 6 players.



=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
- Gameplay and replayability: 3/4. Building railroad tracks and delivering goods is fun. There is not much thought required, but a light game is nice every now and then.        

- Absence of luck: 2/4. Despite of what I read in multiple reviews before buying this game, I feel that the randomness in this game hinders strategy through the "Service Bounties" cards and the random drawing of new goods when urbanizing a new city. Railways of the World is no luck fest, but I do think that the winner will more often than not be the one that had the luck of the draw on his side. 

- Duration: 3/4. A game takes some 2 hours with 3 players.

- Player range: 4/4. Fun with a wide range of players, even with 2.

- Theme and looks: 4/4. This is where the game really shines. The components are great looking and the theme is strong, plausible and very well integrated. The board could have been somewhat better looking and the resources are represented by cubes, but one shouldn't be too demanding :-)   

- Set up/clean up time: 3/4. Less than 10 minutes.

- Learning curve and documentation: 2/4. The rule book is quite confusing, especially because it is separated into a main part and separate parts for the expansions. Also, one has to write his own reference sheet. I wonder what is so difficult about writing good game documentation really?

- Durability: 3/4. None of the components will easily break.

- Portability: 1/4. You'll need a moving van to transport this huge an heavy box. Also be aware that this game requires plains of table space, even when using the small Mexico board.

- Like: great theme and beautiful components, wide player range, nice game mechanics.

- Dislike: a quite pronounced luck factor.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Railroad Tycoon is a streamlined version of [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/metasearch.php?searchtype=game&search=age+of+steam&B1=Go]Age of Steam[/url], which is already a brilliant game by my current favorite designer, Martin Wallace (Glenn Drover from Eagle Games is also credited in this version). Railroad Tycoon is more approachable and forgiving than AoS and has better components (an "almost-too-huge" hand-drawn map, train-themed buildings, sturdy engine cards, and colored trains.) Railroad Tycoon cards serve as hidden victory point triggers. similar to Ticket to Ride "longest track" cards. Railroad Operations cards are drawn at the end of each turn and these are visible to all players. Players can use one of their actions to draw one of these cards, which provide various benefits ("reach destination x" for victory points or "immediately take two actions", for instance). Sometimes these cards can cause a fairly large imbalance/luck factor. For instance, one of the cards provides the player an extra 20 points (!), whereas others provide only 6 points. It's much deeper than Ticket to Ride, but not as deep as Age of Steam. Thematically, it's better than either game. After several plays, players start to learn where the key areas are on the board and will get in there early, in hopes that the proper cards will turn up and convert to some easy points. This isn't an ideal situation, but it doesn't ruin an otherwise good game. Recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I dislike that this version has lost some of the tension of Age of Steam (bidding for special actions, now able to issue shares any time) and it seems far too easy to get cash-flow positive to an AoS veteran.
On the other hand, I've come to like the goal cards after a few more plays. They add another layer of decision making to the relatively simple "build track - shift cubes".
Other changes (scoring track, loco maintenance, etc) don't bother me much whether they're in or out. They just change the stratgies a bit.

Yes its huge and has pretty bits if that also sways your opinion (doesn't do much for me).

RATING: 7

RATING: 5
Lancashire Rails, Volldampf, Age of Steam, Railroad Tycoon.  On it goes - moving coloured cubes over bits of acquired railway line, earn income points, break the spiral of debt.  This is probably the lightest of the four games, and the easiest to "get ahead" with your cash.  A deck of cards throw a little bit of chaos into the mix, and you can't be eliminated by mismanaging your money.  Good fun, fast to play.  Problems?  Several - the game board is way too big; you have to begin in or around the north east, or you will lose and lose badly; why isn't there any 60 degree curves with the game pitched at this scale (the north east will get totally blocked in a few turns); etc.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.25
The much-improved version of the Age of Steam rules.  Streamlines a lot of crap out of the game, fixes the income/vp track, adds in some cards for more interest, and has a far more attractive board/components.  Now you have 3 actions a turn, and for each one you choose from a menu of options.  That's it.  You can issue shares any time, and the start player auction (if you play with the later edition rules) is better too.
It's still a fair amount of work to play.  You've got to stare at the map and identify where the good deliveries are going to be, early and late.  Then you've got to take a look at the bonuses and figure out how to get your hands on at least one (that shot of extra income makes a huge difference in getting your business off the ground).  It's way more enjoyable than its predecessors, but still not something I feel like playing all the time.
Naturally, it must be said that the board is huge (table-space-wise) and also too large for a small-player-count game, in which case you should play one of the expansion maps.
Colorblind note: Depending on the edition, map, and lighting, it can be difficult to tell the dark blue and purple cubes/cities apart.

RATING: 6
I played Railroad Tycoon once. Railroad Tycoon is the better of all the Eagle Games games by a long shot in my opinion.

Which is saying a lot because most of the Eagle Games are garbage in my opinion. I find Railroad Tycoon to be a Age of Steam light. Maybe  the reason why this is a better Eagle Games game is because Martin Wallace has  his input on this game.

Railroad Tycoon is somewhat different than Age of Steam. I say you could own both games. Both games could hit the table for a different game experience for those of you who are a huge fan of train games.

If you want something heavy you'll play Age of Steam. If your a bit tired or don't want to think too much, than Railroad Tycoon can be a nice alternative.

The warp board really sucks. I was thinking that if there were one Eagle Game to have in my collection it would be Railroad Tycoon. Just the fact the board is like that, it's a really a turn off for me and probably won't get it just for that reason.

Those of who likes this game, check into Age of Steam. It's worth your while.

I really find it superior than the other Eagle games I have played. Maybe the fact that Martin had a hand in it affects my judgement a little. I really enjoy Warfrogs games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I waited to rate RRT until I had played it several times. Each play the game got better.

The several complaints about "luck" in the cards are, IMO, bogus. The auction for turn position and the open-ended variety of choices for building track and moving products more than make up for any luck turn-sequence that benefits a single player. Let's not forget, the player who gets lucky and snags a good card has to burn an action to do so. Quit yer bitchin and respond! DO SOMETHING!

In my most recent play with 4 people I overcame a 25 point defecit to catch the leader (who had gotten great cards) and ended up winning the game by 10 points. Even the other two players had pulled to with 10 points of the early leader.

Game length will vary. RRT can freeze some peopless brains into AP mode. So be it, don't play a game that is known to be 2-4 hours in length if you only like 1 hour games. Carping about length is pointless.

As in all the Eagle Games, RRT is true eye-candy. It looks cool, has a significant heft factor that is deeply satisfying and frankly, the game looks incredible when it's about 70% done... with all the locomotives, tracks and empty city pieces on the board.

RRT vs. AOS

This is AOS light for sure. But it will overshadow AOS in the end. It's very accessable to a wider audience. It's about 2,000% better to look at (go ahead, go look at the front and back cover of AOS and tell me which game has more curb appeal), it's more forgiving and therefore more appealing to a larger player base and frankly, I admire Martin Wallace's ability to refine an already good rules set into a game that, if anything, will bring even more people into the fold.

DISCLAIMER ~ I love train games.

RATING: 9
Comments apply to the original "Railroad Tycoon" edition:

Very impressed.  Plays in about 2 hours, which is decent length for a game session, and is easy to learn.  I can see that this would also appeal to non-gamers, and be easy enough to introduce to them.  Using engines to keep track of the score is a little fiddly, but aside from that the components look and work great (as long as you have a table big enough for the massive game board!)  

General rule changes worth adopting from the expansion for all games (as posted [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/527011/the-newest-version-of-railroad-tycoon-and-how-to]here[/url]): 
1. any hex with water costs $3000; 
2. players may [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/92820]choose[/url] 1 of 2 Tycoon cards after the game is set up; 
3. shares can only be taken to get money for immediate use; 
4. blocking is allowed, but players may redirect their last tile (paying the usual cost for that hex, and counting towards the four maximum per turn) - see Keith Blume's post [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/2203326#2203326]here[/url].  
5. [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/307574]Passenger Lines[/url] replaces New Train.  Remove Railroad Executive card that gives an extra turn.
6. Having major lines available to all players from the beginning of the game.  Otherwise it can be somewhat of a lottery if the cards show up in the USA map - perhaps all the major lines should all be revealed at the beginning of the base game with the USA map, as suggested and discussed [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/337154]here[/url].
Many of [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/318095]these variants[/url] are commonly used by experienced RRT players.

See also my comments on Railways of the World.

Status: gifted to a friend (RRT)
Status: girl gaming power (first printing)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Effectively this is Railroad Tycoon with new/better maps, and incorporating the [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/445604]rules tweaks[/url] already implemented in the expansions.  Railroad Tycoon, Mark II - my favourite train game series just got better!  Terrific components, lavish production (looks like a miniature railroad!), accessible yet great economic game-play, strong theme, decent playing time, perfect weight game, highly recommended! See my comprehensive pictorial introduction to this series, which trades the development of the Railroad Tycoon system from Age of Steam onwards, reviews the gameplay, and compares the expansions, here: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/533330

The randomizing of goods cubes at the start of the game is an excellent mechanic for making every game different.  To make the goods less abstract, we assign them as follows: black = coal, yellow = grain, purple = iron, blue = passengers-mail, red = cattle (lumber is other option that would be true to the time, petroleum/oil was first transported in 1865).  

Comes with two maps: Eastern U.S. and Mexico.  See my pictorial review of the Mexico map (great for 2-4 players): http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/522307 (Note: [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/8164022#8164022]play with all cubes on Mexico map regardless of player numbers[/url])

[imageid=1920897 inline] [imageid=1919532 inline]

[thread=1118209][/thread]

The Railroad Tycoon engine just keeps motoring along, and it's great to see this latest incarnation of the game come with further improvements (and with word that there's still more to come).  Overall it's a very good reimplementation, that improves on the original in small and subtle ways, to make a good game even better: improved rules, a new map for less players, tweaked components, and more.  Main changes:
● Additional Mexico map. 
● Smaller US map (and separate income track). 
● Better long term objectives. 
● Improved starting cards.
● Revised and improved rules. 
● Other cosmetic improvements.
With the original and highly popular Railroad Tycoon now out of print, Railways of the World is a solid replacement and all-round improvement, and I hope that more in the boardgaming community will discover what a gem this game really is. For a detailed comparison between Railroad Tycoon and Railways of the World, see my pictorial review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/526996

Also worth considering: AoS style turn order bidding: "In Railroad Tycoon, the turn order auction has only the winner paying money and going first, with the other players following in clockwise order. In AoS style bidding, players bid for turn order as in the official rules but the resulting turn order is the reverse of the order in which players drop out of the bidding (last man standing goes first). Players who do not bid anything go after all those who do, retaining their turn order relative to each other.  Players who do not bid, and also the first player to drop out after bidding, pay nothing. The last and second-to-last players pay their full bids. Everybody in between pays half their bid, rounded up."  This is a For Sale style bidding system; alternatively you can have all players going free except the top two bidders, since in RRT the difference between going 2rd vs 5th is usually not that important. For more discussion, see [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/315955]here[/url] and [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/289885]here[/url].  
The latest edition includes rules for this as a variant in the rulebook.  These rules are also implemented in Railways Through Time, as discussed [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/642588/questions-about-the-new-rules-for-the-turn-order-a]here[/url].  A Vegas Showdown style bidding also available [filepage=43455]here[/filepage] as recommended and discussed [thread=1002686]here[/thread].

Two player variants on the Eastern US map: games also seem to work fine, there's less interaction perhaps, and you're both building up your own empire, but it's still fun.  Two solutions to create interaction:
a)  [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/100656]the popular 2 player variant using only the middle board segment[/url] (which provides 2 of each color city) - any hexes which are not complete are not playable and are not considered (including Pittsburgh and Atlanta).  Need to playtest to determine if the full number of goods are used, or if the reduced number as usual for 2 players.  
b) Glenn Drover suggests a [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/1242766#1242766]two player variant[/url] where neither player may build west of the mountains until one reaches 60 points, to help solve the problem of imbalance in the north-east.

Rule notes:
- Score track wrapping: see [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/article/6659792#6659792]this post[/url] (and the following one) about how wrapping on the score track is supposed to work.
- Passing: can you pass on a round? [url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/19906055#19906055]Official answer: Yes.[/url]
- Blocking: see [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/article/20238999#20238999]this post[/url] and the following one.

Also owned: 

[gameid=87627] - Mexico map for 2 players, comes with base game
[gameid=72267] - Western US map, includes rotor cities and fuel depots that can be used on other maps
[gameid=42964] - English map, alternate map, and optional advanced game with shares.
[gameid=133412] - reimplements English map and removes wonky advanced game.
[gameid=30450] - European map, has less cities, a smaller but higher quality board, seems to result in a tighter and more balanced game with tougher choices, and a better choice for 2 and 3 player games.  It is optional to reduce cubes in 2/3 player games with the expansion, [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/2569511#2569511]as discussed here[/url], at least with three players.  
[gameid=131386] - Canada map, plus rules for transatlantic game
[gameid=72268] - Travel across different maps, the number used is proportional to the number of players.

Best with 2-3 players: Mexico map (plus Europe & Canada map with reduced cubes); best with 3-4 players: Europe & England & Canada maps; best with 4+ players: Eastern & Western US maps; Railways Through Time works for all player numbers.

[gameid=99692] - random events that apply from turn to turn.

Estimated playing time: allow 30-45 minutes per player if they know what they are doing, 45-75 minutes if they don't.

Reduced cubes for 2/3 player games: do NOT reduce cubes on Mexico, Europe, England, or Time maps.  DO reduce cubes on Canada map (though arguably [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/13169039#13169039]optional[/url]).  See also [url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/21919224#21919224]this overview[/url] and this [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/article/23545547#23545547]official one[/url].

Info about a VASSAL module [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/567700/railways-online-play-via-vassal-available-and-an-o]here[/url].

FROM THE COMPENDIUM

Recommended Player Counts

(NB: You can also use this chart as a guide to fine-tune your games to your preferred playing style. Smaller player counts lower direct competition, while larger player counts will result in a more cutthroat game. Interestingly, Railways Through Time allows you to simply adjust the number of boards you use to achieve the same effect.)

2-Player
•Mexico
•Eastern U.S. with Glenn Drover Variant
•Railways through Time

3-Player
•Mexico
•North America
•Europe
•Railways through Time

4-Player
•North America
•Great Britain
•Europe
•Railways through Time

5-Player
•Eastern U. S.
•Western U.S.
•Europe
•Great Britain

6-Player
•Eastern U.S.
•Western U.S.

Cube Reduction

Cube Reduction for Fewer Players:
Some expansion maps do not require cube reduction for fewer players. Railways of Europe was designed to play well with fewer players and many feel Railways of Great Britain also plays better without cube reduction.

Cube Reduction Chart:
MAP....................2-player.......3-player
Eastern U.S.	........Reduce.........Reduce
Western U.S.	.......Reduce.........Reduce
Mexico	...........Don’t Reduce....Don’t Reduce
Europe	...........Don’t Reduce....Don’t Reduce
Great Britain	.......Reduce........Reduce
North America	.....Reduce........Reduce
Through Time ....Don’t Reduce...Don’t Reduce

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
RRT is a great game that becomes more interesting and competitive as the number of players increases.  Five or six is the sweet spot, but we enjoy it with any number, even with some of the two player variants.

Balanced growth is key.  The rules are relatively simple so this game is accessible to most newcomers , but it does take a while to really understand the flow of the game.  One also needs a couple of plays to understand the relative value and merits of the Tycoon and operation cards, which can be crucial to winning.  Consequently, the experienced player will almost always win, though newbies usually do well enough to enjoy the game.

Railroad Tycoon can be played friendly or competitively, but in general the competition gets more intense with more players.  With more experienced players, this can be a brutal game.

RRT is a fantastic game and I will never pass on a chance to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I've played three games with the same group, and the youngest player has gotten a land grant every time right off the bat and won the game (also by heading to the Northeast :) ). This is easy to fix, though.

RATING: 8.5
As a longtime Age of Steam fan, and as someone who has generally been turned off by Eagle Games, I had no real desire to ever try this take on the Age of Steam system.  But I enjoyed it quite a bit more than I was expecting to.  There are still some of Eagle's garish design choices, meaningless components, and a slight non-elegant feel to the game, but overall, I really liked it.  It does away with AoS's auctions, but adds some interesting cards that provide a whole new dimension (and some might say slight chaos) to the experience.  I feel like it simplified the rules and made it more forgiving while still giving each player almost as many decisions to make.  The fact that the "Railways of the World" edition comes with two maps right out of the base game box is a plus too.  Now that my kids have been exposed to this version of the system, I think I'll be playing Age of Steam less, and this more.

RATING: 6
I really like economic games where one has to build an empire, so I was bound to like this one too. It is well balanced and one needs to make some tough decisions during the play. The problem is that the game feels a bit monotonous for me. In my opinion, the influence one has on other players is not enough and the choices one needs to make don't really change as the game progresses. I also really don't like the look of the empty city markers on the board.
Still, I had some plays with this which I really enjoyed. The game is quite long and in the meantime the situation of a company can change a lot. It is fun to observe a railroad network, which seemed to be the strongest in the first half of the game, to slowly fall apart in the second. Although this does not happen too often, but even the possibility of something like this happening speaks for the potential of the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
RT has a big plus in that it really feels like you are building tracks across the map for the purposes of shipping goods. I love the shares / debt balancing mechanic and the simple turn structure that keeps the game flowing. However, I can't shake how unbalanced the map is. I think it is by design: players are supposed to fight for the most populous areas and then expand out of there. Unfortunately, I find this concept is lost on new players and also severely structures a player's first number of turns - with the outcome being based mostly on best play and luck from the Railroad cards. From an advantageous point, a player seems to be able to expand out and keep a steady lead - unless another player is able to make some pretty good moves, again, based on card luck and precise turns. This is one of the few games I've played which I've enjoyed, but because I see how the play will go, I can't imagine wanting to invest the hours that a solid game of this takes. It has a few really good ideas that I think can be found in other economic games and that play in less time and with a less ridged opening game strategy. Even with the Railways of Europe expansion (where the map is more balanced) I was able to take the upper portion of the map. Nobody fought me for it and I ended up having lots of money early on while the rest of the players struggled to just pay off their debt. I do think they could have gone about it a different way to balance my lead - but the point is it would take another couple of plays to get to this harmonious point and I just think there are better games out there to spend my time with. Ultimately, I don't think this game should be played with less than 4 players, and I don't want to invest the 2.5-3 hours that will take for a game that might create a certain lead halfway in and there doesn't seem to be enough to balance a runaway in that regard. I reserve solid judgement because I haven't played the game enough - but I don't think I'll hang on to it. Might enjoy AoS or Steam as an alternative!

Update August 2007

July 2011
