=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Age of Steam didn't wow me like it wowed others, so I have been curious to try Railroad Tycoon.  I liked Railroad Tycoon, but now I wonder if I miss role selection in AoS.  It's now been a while since I played AoS, but it does seem like Railroad Tycoon is more forgiving.  Even so, If you get behind in either game early on, you are pretty much doomed for the rest of the game, and there really isn't anything one can do to catch up unless you luck out with cards or something.  Or you can spend the time just pecking at other players.  The Eagle production looks to my like an example of aiming high, but with less than stellar results.  You've got a strange mix of nice and shoddy in one giant box.  The watertowers are fun, but the board basically stinks.  It's too big, and even though it is too big, the score track is too small and easy to mess up.  The artwork with the rivers is unclear, the city colors of blue and purple are hard to tell apart, and the board doesn't lie flat.  So, they had great intentions and aimed high, but in doing so had to cheapen component quality and make a game that is in the end is expensive. I do look forward to trying the next generation of this game, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Bought in February 2012. I first played this at a board game club while we were briefly living in San Francisco, and I instantly fell deeply in love.

When I was a kid my parents used to think I loved trains, mainly because I was obsessed with my Brio set. But trains didn't interest me, what I loved was BUILDING TRACK. Trying to use every piece to create a huge fully interconnected network without creating any loops that you could get stuck in. Pushing the trains down the track was merely a mildly pleasant chore - like the second half of a round of [thing=31481][/thing] - to test the quality of the creation.

And this game lets me turn track building into a competition. I LOVE IT!

I find the wonderfully overdone aesthetics of this game irresistible. Yeah, the board is too big to fit on any human-sized table; yeah, the empty city markers are ridiculous, but I LOVE IT.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Railroad Tycoon is, in my opinion, the best effort from Eagle games to date.  This is largely due to the fact that the system has been adapted from Martin Wallace’s stellar Age of Steam.  The changes are, for the most part, acceptable, and some are even quite good.  The result is a game that is often tense, and forces players to make important decisions throughout its duration.  While dominating the northeast can lead to victory, several players should be competing in this profitable area, making that strategy less likely to succeed with experienced players.  The result is that there are numerous strategies to pursue, and no one sure path to victory.

Is the game as good as Age of Steam?  That will certainly depend upon who you ask, and perhaps what you are seeking.  Experienced gamers will likely prefer the purity and tightness of Age of Steam, while folks who enjoy games with a bit more randomness and freedom may well find Railroad Tycoon more palatable.  Both are quite good, though, and should please the majority of folks.

RATING: 7
Overall, a very good game with some implementation problems.  Its very simple for how deep the game is, and that is its strength.  It also rewards effective long term planning.  The problems are in the map design, the implementation of the secondary mechanics, and the resulting balance issues.  Certain areas must be fought over or you give someone the game.  In other areas, if you leave someone alone, you give them the game.  I prefer just looking out for how I can win and screwing other players because I want to - not because I have to do it to keep the game from going off the rails (excuse the pun).  Spending part of the game chasing down what other people are doing and worrying about whether we let someone do too much - not fun.  The cards are obnoxious (both tycoon and railroad operations).  I'd like to give it another play sometime, but I'm not too excited about it.  I really wanted to like this, but I just don't think train games are my thing.  Martin Wallace is also not really my thing.  I like the idea of this game, I don't think its bad, I can recognize the good things about the design and its appeal, but I just didn't have that much fun with it.  I've had a similar experience with other Martin Wallace games.  I should probably give it a second chance.

RATING: 10
This game is easy to learn - rulebook is 4-5 pages.  This is a solid step up from gateway games (for instance Ticket to Ride, Airlines Europe), but still a game that new players will understand very quickly with some guidance. Every turn there are so many options and you have to balance short term gain with long term strategy. This requires planning, but also you will have to predict what the other players are up to. Timing is essential, and being able to control the triggering of game end is often the deciding factor in tight games. 

The Mexico map is excellent for 2 players, although I would suggest to skip the turn order auction in the 2 player game. The Eastern US map is somewhat unbalanced, but is probably best with 5 or 6 players. The England and Wales, as well as the Europe map is excellent for 3-4 players. 

Railways of the World has almost an epic feeling with the large maps and the wonderful high quality components. The huge boards look fantastic as tracks, trains and empty city markers are starting to appear. The US cross-continental rules will be released in the summer of 2013, making it possible to combine the Eastern ann Western US map. 

After a few plays, you will start to understand the possible strategies. After all,this is not a very deep, complicated game. With the right gaming group, you will feel the challene and tension in every game, and the game somehow seems to provide more fun and excitement than some other train games (e.g. Steam, Age of Steam).  

The random distribution of cubes during set-up contributes to every game playing out differently.Highly recommended for casual gamers who are seeking new challenges and more serious gamers. I enjoy train games a lot, everything from this one to the 18x family games

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[Initial Impression: 8]
Comparisions with its "parent" game, Age of Steam, are inevitable, but the two games are really geared toward different audiences - those who find Age of Steam too harsh, haven't played it, or are willing to set aside the comparision will be delighted by the scope, theme, and playability of this game.  There are some issues with the production, all highly discussed in the forums, but none sufficient to diminish the game experience.  Perhaps the only one that really affects gameplay regards which side of a river some city exits are on. The auction for player order is a bit odd, since it is generally better for the person on your right to win than for you to win (unless there is a very important operation card available). You don't need to worry about issuing too many shares, as using them to get a large engine can be rapidly made up for by long deliveries.  The operation cards add a bit of spice (or a bit of mayhem, depending upon your view), but knowing the card effects in advance allows them to be incorporated into your plans, making them almost predictable (and never surprising).  The large map can allow players to not interfere with one another if they wish, but the major lines will still usually see competition.  I really enjoy the heavy (some say enjoyably punishing) feel of Age of Steam, but find Railroad Tycoon to be more accessible, intuitive, and thematic in a shorter playing time.

RATING: 10
3-4-13
I had a number of old comments, but I changed my rating to a 10 now.  The scoring system is simple and elegant and ties in well with money.  The debt management is tense at first, and player order auctioning can be very crucial to a certain card.  Running the numbers of profitability are important, but later on the plan changes to pushing more points.  It's an interesting dynamic.  Having the goods randomized each game means there will be different opportunities, so despite playing the same map, there can be a lot of different nuances to the way things play out.

It plays great with 2 players on the Mexico map, which is a boon for playing only with my partner.  It's light enough for anyone to understand, but intricate enough to have a lot of strategy and options each turn.  I keep looking forward to playing this more, and for the foreseeable future, this will be a 10.

RATING: 6.5
After on solo play, and comparing to both Steam and AoS, I have to say I like this the least of the three.

Less a gamer's game, more a family game, with lovely bits and a big pretty look.

Still has the same very random good growth of AoS (I like Steams' control better), and what's worse the Shares pit doesn't take you cleanly out of the game, but leaves you peddling madly in air, unable to fall to your immediate doom, and stuck taking shares all the way to the end of the game (one player ended up with 49 shares, but still had a positive victory point total in the end).

I think the bigger board decreases some of the competition (with 4 tycoons), and the random fall of cards can hurt you if your bidding options are not the best they come out.

But all said, not a bad game, just not as great as it's older and younger siblings. A 6.5 to start (1 solo play).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Comment for [b]Railroad Tycoon[/b] (Aug 2008):

This gives the feeling that you are actually building a railroad network. Although there are some standard "openings", the random cube distribution gives each game a different feel. It's fast to play--almost no downtime.

Update: this may not be a good 'gateway' game. It is easy to learn, and fast and fun to play. However, the newbie will be overwhelmed and lost as to what to do, as he/she will not appreciate the strategies and tactics (which require several playings to understand). If playing with experienced players, the newbie will be devastated in terms of scoring, and possibly demoralized to the point he/she hates boardgaming in general. It's best to play with players of roughly equal experience or at least familiar with some boardgaming. A neophyte may get the wrong impression. Rating: "10".

Update: introduced this to a new gaming couple on a Game Day, after we had suffered through 4 hrs of BSG. They had played Trans-America prior to that thinking that was too simple, but after playing RRT, they loved it! Just the right complexity level, so I guess it depends on the new player.

Update (09/06/09) after 22 plays: After playing Steam and Age of Steam, I've decided I like RRT the best, and Steam in second place. I want to burn my copy of AOS. I've also played Chicago Express and 1870 (and another 18xx game), and prefer them over AOS also, with Chicago Express somewhat more preferable to Steam.

AOS feels like programming a spreadsheet. The visual appeal of AOS is severely lacking when compared to RRT, or even Steam. At least Steam has two different "flavors"--basic and standard. I like the basic version better, while Standard is just the same as AOS--a big, painful experience. Haven't tried Standard yet, but don't want to bother subjecting myself to that kind of torture again. So, there is an element of randomness in RRT, but life is chaos, isn't it? You need to be able to react to some of the random, but somewhat predictable nature of the cards. Cosmic Encounter is way too random. RRT's "randomness" is just right.

Update (08/06/10): After playing a bunch of Empire Builder crayon-rail games, Chicago Express, a smattering of some Winsome train games, and a touch of 18XX for an extended period, this was a nostalgic homecoming experience. Because the board is fixed, there are 'standard openings' and  strategies, unlike the huge variability offered by Empire Builder and its siblings. Chicago Express and 18XX are more of an economic game, as they don't give the feeling of running a train--they actually feel like running a company. Empire Builder, et al. are more solitary, racing around the board completing contracts, and interaction/conflict is minimal. RRT is somewhere in between of running a train, and developing a network, without the complex economics introduced by a stock market mechanism.

Update (01/04/2014) 37 plays: It has been awhile since we've played this. However, 3-player is not recommended. The board is too big, and there is not enough constraints or tension. The north-east should not be left alone to a single player--duh. However, this leaves the third player to run wild elsewhere. RRT is best with five or six. The game is starting to wane with me and others in my group, since people complain that it is too long with six. With three, this is a "7".

Comment for [b]Railways of the World[/b] (Nov 2012):

Component-wise and rules-wise, not much different from RRT, with several changes:
1) Board is a touch smaller.
2) Slight modifications on map--New York has 3 exits, instead of 2. New Orleans to Mobile is 4 hexes instead of 5 hexes.
3) Score track on separate board. Minor quibble where score track has "switchbacks", where it zig-zags. Very faint arrows show how one track leads into the next, but this is very easy to overlook and screw up the scoring.
4) Operation cards have been tweaked. All major routes are now available on a first-come, first-scored basis, instead of waiting for a card to show up. The 4-speed train is now replaced with a "deliver and collect four different goods cubes for 4 VP" card.
5) Building track in water now costs $3000. In RRT, the cost to [i]cross[/i] a river was an additional $1000, on top of the $2000 for building in a plains hex.

Printing quality has somewhat improved, but it is still hard to distinguish the blue and purple cities. Whether playing RRT or RWotW, both editions rate a '10'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is essentially the same as RRT, with a number of exceptions:
1) The map is significantly smaller, so it is playable on a 4 ft diameter round table! RRT requires at least a 5 ft table or a longer, rectangular table. This is huge.
2) The map is a one-piece foldout.
3) There are a number of changes to the map:
3a) New Orleans to Mobile is now 3 hexes instead of 5.
3b) New York now has 4 exits instead of three.
3c) Mountains are delineated by a white dot.
3d) The Ozark Mountains are now present.
3e) The blue cities are still blue, but the purple cities are now pink/fuchsia.
4) The score track has its own board now.
5) No more river crossing; rivers are now considered water hexes at $3,000.
6) Some cleaned up rules with bonds issuing (formerly called stocks).
7) Some Railroad Tycoon cards have slightly different objectives. You also can choose between two at the beginning of the game.
8) The plastic train pieces are a matt finish plastic--blue is now horrific sky blue. Black is now gray. Yellow is now mustard colored. Purple is now pink. Ugh.
9) The black start player wooden train has been replaced by a card.
10) The goods cubes are smaller. The colors don't necessarily match with the city colors, however.
11) The four-train public objective card has been replaced with the four-kinds-of-goods "passenger" card, similar to Railways of Europe.
12) Rule for making all major-lines objectives available is optional.
13) Comes with a 2-4 player Mexico board.

The only serious issue I have with this version is the score board: it is not functional or intuitive. Our first game was ruined when the scoring trains were incorrectly placed when moving to the second track. This meant there was a $6,000 jump from $18,000 to $24,000 in income. Only later did we notice that the scoring snaked back and forth like switchbacks. The direction arrows were in white, faded, and easy to overlook. A better solution would have been to go around in a circle, like in Railways of Europe.

The lack of color coordination between trains, cities, and cubes issue continues to boggle the mind.

Also, it is ridiculous that they would be so cheap as to revert to a start player card instead of a wooden token. It is easy to get it mixed up with the operation cards and bond certificates.

I would seriously consider getting this game, just so I could play RRT on my existing table, but I'm considering getting a 5 ft table in the near future, so this is redundant. The quality is a touch better than RRT, in terms of card stock feel, but the color coordination issues means there's still room for improvement.

RATING: 9
After a single play (sans cards) of [i]Railroad Tycoon[/i], I was hooked.  A great medium-weight train game that looks fantastic and plays fast with low rules-overhead.

After 6 plays of [i]Railways[/i] (5x 2p (no cards), 1x 4p (with cards), all on the Mexico map):

The cards (Barons & Operations) add flavor and chaos into the mix.  With the cards you have a highly thematic family (or casual) game that's still meaty at the core; without the cards the game becomes a tighter, more vicious mathematical pissing contest, akin to [i]There Will Be Blood[/i] the boardgame, with plenty of forehead-vein-popping "I drink [i]your[/i] milkshake" moments.

I have no experience with the parent game ([gameid=4098]) beyond looking at pictures on the Internet and recoiling at how ugly it is—of course, this means it's a Very Serious Game, since everyone knows the ugliest games are the most rigorous and stern.  I like my math to be fun, so, yeah, it was the pretty bits that finally pulled me in... but the gameplay is solid.

[floatleft][imageid=1453104 small][/floatleft]
Painting the "Empty City" markers really kicks the visual presentation over the top and makes the game feel like playing with a tiny model train set.  Much like the satisfying diorama you're left with at the end of [thing=31260][/thing]*, the board is beautiful after a frenzied game of financial ruination and axe handle hobo-beatings.

RATING: 8
Very enjoyable - a big table is needed for this one. 

Game provides three types of crossover tracks that are never the ones you need! A potential house rule is to treat a crossover track of any type as viable for any crossing of two links - ie ignore the actual tile detail. 

Game works better when you categorise a western link city as multi-coloured, once the link is created. This makes the western link much more valuable as a delivery point for all players.

Another variation that lenghtens the game but adds greatly to the tactical play is to retain all cubes on the board except those delivered between cities of the same colour.

RATING: 8
The game had terrible production/design values - large board with tons of wasted space, scoring pieces too big for the scoring track (which is on the edge of the board and is constantly knocked), many boards warped, empty city markers were far too big, etc. etc. etc.

However, ignoring the design faults (and the hidden investor cards), you've got a great game of railway shipping that's easy to teach and play.  The game ending is slightly artificial - the game ends when all the goods run out - and players create railroads that then serve no purpose.  However, it's always a pleasure to play, there are enough decisions to make to keep players interested and none of the decisions are overwhelming.

Find a big table, give it a whirl, and you should find a good game emerges - just don't think too hard about the theme.

If you let someone get a monopoly on New York, you deserve to lose the game.

RATING: 5
Not an /awful/ game, but it lacks the bite of AoS. Being able to issue shares at any time makes a huge difference, and I find the role selection much better than the cards, both due to the randomness of which cards come up, and due to having to spend an action to take one. It is also a pity that seating order can matter so much. Having no knowledge of what cubes will appear when taking the production action makes it a lot weaker than its AoS equivalent.

Unlike AoS, the board is so vast that (apart from racing for certain cards) you are likely to only interact with 1, maybe 2 other players in a 6 player game.

The components are either not great (e.g. the board warp problems, and it is hard to tell from some of the art what type of terrain a hex is meant to be, and in isolation the blue colour could be confused with purple) or ridiculously over-produced (e.g. the plastic "empty hex" markers). The rules are also rather vague.

RATING: 6.5
While Railroad Tycoon is an enjoyable pick-up and deliver/tile-laying railroad game – one that works quite well with beginner gamers - it is lacking something for me.  Over the course of about five plays, I have found that it really doesn’t have any real strong strategic decisions in it.  You have a limited number of actions to choose from, but choosing a particular action is never a tough decision.  Combine the lack of tough decisions with a map that allows players to stay in their own corner of the world for most of the game and I feel that the game just falls flat.

RATING: 9.5
This game is really one of the best I've played. There is so much strategy, so much interaction. Even when I know I'm going to lose, I still have a blast playing it. I love how much strategizing you have to do to stay ahead, yet there is still a fair bit of room for opportunism. Should you play the long game or go for a short term reward? The bluffing element and territorial aspects keeps it competitive, while the way you populate maps keeps it fresh every time you play. I know this is one of those games that might scare away a non-gamer, but I think everyone should try it at least once. It's really a quite simple game to understand.

RATING: 8
This is a fantatsic game.  The components are phenomenal (with exception to the board) and the gameplay is just strategic enough without the rules clutter and analysis paralysis that is usually involved.  Having more players ups the ante and unlike Conquest of the Empire (a game from the same publisher with shared mechanics), the cards are not entirely too numerous and random.  The scoring track is brilliant as it seems to prevent runaway leader and keep a nice tight end game.  Very solid.

Update:  You really need a full compliment for this game as it doesn't scale well at all.  The cards per turn are also really variable in terms of reward and the secret objectives can range from impossible to way too easy.  I think Age of Steam is the better game when the day is over (with a few tweaks, mind you).

RATING: 9.75
This one is a big winner.  My personal favorite "big" game.

The Tycoon Cards, Railroad Operations Cards and the random distribution of the cubes at the beginning of the game add to a very healthy re-playability.

As for the map choices:

- Eastern U.S. (Railroad Tycoon version) - great map to play.  Has color issues with blue / purple cities.  Sweet spot is 3 to 5 players, although the map can be a bit tough to find that 5th good starting location depending on cube distribution.  Map generally plays with players taking on 2 to 12 shares.

- Railways of the Eastern U.S. (Railways of the World version) - the Railways of the World version of the Eastern U.S. map "fixed / improved" a couple of items with the Railroad Tycoon version.  Color issues are "fixed".  Shortening the distance between New Orleans and Mobile make it a much more viable starting location, which enables the game to handle 5 or 6 players better.  The Railways of the World rule mod having the major lines available at game start also is an improvement.

- [gameid=30450] - Terrific map.  Prepare to take more shares to fund building over mountainous terrain - I generally have seen 5 to 12 shares required.  Sweet spot is 3 to 4 players on this map.  

- [gameid=42964] is another terrific expansion.  The increased density of cities and reduced number of mountains makes this expansion play quicker than either the Eastern U.S. or Europe maps.  I see this map as a good 3 to 5 player option.  Without a lot of mountains and shorter distances between cities, think 2 to 8 shares for each player.

- [gameid=72267] - Great alternative to the Eastern U.S. maps if you want to play a "big" map.  The new Western U.S. rules (i.e., spin dials on the cities & refueling stations) are a bit "wonky" - we generally don't use them.  Map appears to play 3 to 5 players comfortably and a 6th player is certainly possible, as there are many viable starting locations (depending on cube distribution).

- [gameid=87627] - Terrific learning map.  Mountainous, so players will be taking shares.  3 players appears to be the sweet spot for the map.  This is the only map I would consider playing with 2.  I think 4 would be too tight.

- Recently purchased [gameid=72268].  The theme is quite odd, but the use of maps scales really well to different numbers of players.  I have not tested it at the extremes, but this may be the one expansion that can handle 2 through 6 players.  Acquisition of shares during play seems to be light for this expansion.  Not that it should surprise anyone, but game play on this map is the most different - lots of room for creative play, less opportunities for blocking lines.

- Received [gameid=131386] via Kickstarter.  It's a very solid map.  One of the counter-intuitive aspects is that it actually appeared to be easier to build North-South on this map than East-West.  Acquisition of shares would seem to be nominally in the 2 to 8 range, but could go higher for special strategies/barons.  It felt like 3 - 4 players were right for this map.  I believe it would be possible, but tough, to find that reasonable 5th starting location.

- We have been able to play the Transcontinental game once . . . it's a big undertaking.  I thought the ruleset worked well.  I felt like there was some strategy to making sure you were on the "fat" (i.e., $25/turn) part of the income track when the Western US map came into play . . . enabled you to have cash to build track right away when the new territory opened up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My first Martin Wallace train game.  The more I think on it though, the more I enjoyed the track building and the point-to-point delivery system.  There's definitely that touch of magic here.

I've since picked up the game and even acquired a nice printing of the Unofficial Europe map.  It makes for an enjoyable family game when you drop the auction and only use reasonably balance cards.

Apr 08: Wow, I made it up to 7 playings of this already?  Impressive for such a beast of a game.  Still, the inefficient routes just kill my inner economist.

Rails of Europe Version:

Jun08(1 Play): I usually don't rate expansions, but I view this as an almost completely new game.  It just fixes so much of the RRT glitches.

1. The whole board is used since almost anywhere can be viable.
2. It trims about 30 minutes off the playtime through reduced income and quicker city emptying.
3. It offers an auction track so you can play it with gamers and balance the turn order.
4. The card deck is more balanced; it's missing the stupid double action cards from the original.

All of this adds up to an awesome game.  I really like the network planning and economic efficiency aspects.  The promotion of inefficient "loopy" routes still rubs me wrong on simulation, but I can live with that.  It's also very unforgiving for differences in skill levels.  Newbies just don't stand a chance.

If you at all liked RRT, I think this expansion is worthy.

RATING: 9
I know a lot of gamers prefer Steam/Age of Steam over this, but for me Railways of the World is an almost perfect medium-weight game.

This game actually inspired me to give Steam a try, and Age of Steam another try, but I just don't find extra fun in the extra brain-burn that those games offer. I am amendable to using the auction system from Steam in RotW, though. 

I have to admit that components are a factor in my love of this game. The blue/purple color problem in Railroad Tycoon and RotW 1st ed. used to bother me -- until I managed to trade for the 2nd ed.

The thing that holds this game back from being a 10 for me is how front-loaded the decisionmaking is. Having the very first turn be the most important in the game (asses the entire board and the cards, now determine what those are worth in the turn order auction) is just not great design. I'm tempted to try some variant where the cards and cubes come out at a gradual pace over the first few turns. 

Oh, also, I was a slow learner in this game. Took me 10+ plays before I finally won a game.

RATING: 8.2
Playable with 2, but I would recommend at least 4.  I would like to try it with 5 players next.  Great train game just a notch below its brother Steam.  One complaint:  The turn order auction gives one player the right to act first, but the remaining players act clockwise from the auction winner.  This allows a player who pays nothing to arbitrarily act second.  Therefore, seating arrangement has a big impact on the results of the game.  If you sit left of a player who prefers not to bid on turn order, you are in trouble.  Suggest using a variant similar to the AoS method of bidding for turn order.  Rating with variant: 8.4.  Rating without variant: 8

RATING: 8.5
initial rating....

I liked this quite a bit, it feels a bit lighter than AOS without taking anything away really (except the steam coming out of your ears!) but still a gamers game. Certainty more approachable and much much more forgiving (especially economically) but this isn't without it's own negatives. I would of liked to have seen more balance in the map, that is more useful areas to build in than just the east coast, especially north east but it may not be much of a factor, more plays will tell. Another subtraction is the added luck in the cards, especially the route cards. Having a route card come out after the half-way point in the game is just dumb, this just becomes a random bonus for someone that doesn't have to do anything at this point. I will insist on the following change in my next playing; all route cards to be used in the game are to be in the starting line up of cards, then they can be picked and worked towards, much better IMO. Other than that it was a fun engaging game that will probably get more play than AOS.

RATING: 8.1
A railway infrastructure building game where players generate income by connecting cities to meet supply and demand requirements.  This is a straightforward game, that integrates fairly simplistic economic strategy (i.e., how deep in debt should I go to make this connection?) with area control mechanics (i.e., what routes should I build?).  This is a pleasant, uncomplicated game with very nice components. 

Rating: 8.1 (Fun 8; Gameplay 7; Components 10; Theme 8)

Formula:
[Weighted rating = (((Fun*4) + (Gameplay*3) + (Components *2) + (Theme *1))/10)]

RATING: 9
What I like:
The components are beautiful (it's fun seeing the track spread across the board), the gameplay is tense and well-balanced, and the variable setup makes each game feel very different. Appropriate strategies are non-obvious without being obtuse; it's not easy to find the best move, but I don't feel like I'm floundering aimlessly, either. There might be a bit more luck in the operations cards than suits some people (especially when the major lines are separated from the rest of the deck), but it works well for me and my game group. I haven't played anything else in the Age of Steam family, so this is unlike anything else in my collection and I love it.

What I don't like:
Not very much. Umm, the board is so big that it forces me to stand up a lot to see what's going on? There are a few rules ambiguities, but those have either been neatly cleaned up through the FAQ on BGG or can be easily house ruled as long as the rulings are consistently applied.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Last time I played Railroad Tycoon: The Boardgame (as the game was introduced to me) was years ago. Sadly I don’t remember much about the game.

I remember the components were very well done, perhaps a bit overproduced and I also remember I didn’t like the cards, way too random for me. Both games are superior in all regards but component wise.

If I play the game again, I will come beck to do a proper comment. For now I’m rating it according to my current desire to play it (low).

RATING: 5
I've played one game each of the introductory game on the Mexico map and the full game on the Eastern United States map.  For the introduction game money seemed pretty tight and while overall not a terribly difficult game it took me a little while to strike a balance between how many bonds to take out and when to wait and accumulate the cash.  As it was the intro game wasn't to shabby.  Then played a 6 person with the orginal rule set...for me it took a little long for what it was. Granted it was a learning experience for three of the players and the other two of the three weren't exactly experienced at the game.  I'd like to play a 4 person game...I would play it with more but would prefer that more of the people were experienced to keep the time down (the game went over 4 hours).  

Overall not a bad game...again given that the game was a learning expeirence for 50% of the people playing in might not be fair for me to rate it.  Some cons were that it seemed after about 2 and a half hours there was a clear cut winner.  Also some of the colors on the board take a little while to get use too.  The scoring mat could have been designed differently (It's not necessarily intuitive).  The pros still outway the cons in this game though.  The components are wonderful, replayabilty is pretty high in that the goods cubes are randomized as well as the operational cards, and the scheer number of pathes to victory prevents an optimal way to win.  I'll reevaluate the game after a few more plays but for the time being it goes down as a solid game for  me.

RATING: 8
I find the mechanics similar Martin Wallace's earlier railroad games but, thankfully, without the brain burning complexity which completely turned me off Age of Steam.  If your group loves AoS, stay away... otherwise, Railroad Tycoon will become THE train game of choice for most groups.

We played a 6 player game in a little over 2 hours, and we were learning the rules as we went along, so I feel that with practiced play, a game should not take more than 90-120 minutes.

I enjoyed the action selection mechanism and the random card draws at the start of each turn.  Even though, like AoS, the game penalized players for doing well (although not to the same extent), I don't think that anyone in the game felt that they were completely out of contention during the entire game... and in the end the high and low scores were separated by only 11 points.

This game clears up most of the problems that I had with Age of Steam... and the components are just [i]icing on the cake[/i].  Unless it's the only game on the table, I doubt that I'll play AoS ever again.

My biggest gripe, if you can call it that, is that if you are familiar with the Railroad Tycoon Computer game and were expecting something similar, you will be very disappointed... it is NOTHING like the PC version of the game.   If more of the elements that made the PC version likeable were added back into the mix, I'd probably rate this even higher.  A solid '8.'

RATING: 5.5
Hmmm ... played 4 times now and am still not sure about this one. I have found that it can also get quite repetative - build track, deliver, build track, deliver etc etc.  The income track is counter-intuitive, as your income goes down after you reach a certain amount of VPs - this is obviously a catch-up mechanism, but its very heavy-handed.  Board is a bit too big - its hard to find a table where the board fits AND you have enough space for your bits on the side as well.  Chicago Express is probably the better train game - it certainly plays much quicker!

RATING: 4
After my first play (summer 08) I rated this a weak seven. 

New comment as of 01/09:

My second play has both boosted and undermined my rating. The second time through, I found this to be a remarkably simple game. You spend money to make money. You do it in cycles of three moves. You issue between 2 and 5 shares of stock, depending on the specifics of your acceleration. The first time it was a bit disorienting, perhaps just the hustle and bustle of learning a new game with a huge, busy map and five players. The second time (months later), it all seemed so straightforward. Stake out some territory, look at the goods distribution in each city (random at the start of the game), and then build track, ship goods, and upgrade trains in a steady rhythm. Every now and then the available bonus cards might be worth pausing your steady advance; often they are not. Each player has a secret objective, and I'm not sure they're appropriately balanced. The game needs more incentives to build in the West, particularly the Southwest. Perhaps Dallas could count as two colors for example. Or perhaps land grants used in the southwest quadrant of the board could cover up to six hexes of track. Perhaps the first player to connect to Dallas could get a free train upgrade (who knows why, historically). These are all options that add balance without the altering the system, but it strikes me that the system itself is too mundane for it to matter much.   

Not a bad game, and one I'll probably play one or two more times. I'm just not sure where the excitement is supposed to be coming from. I should try Rails of Europe for the map. The current map seems enormous with three players... but with four or five it's almost like there are two sets of players participating in separate games until the last turn or two. 

Also, I wish Eagle games had spent extra money making the various wooden cubes shaped like different types of goods, rather than including lame and superfluous plastic pieces to mark empty cities. 

UPDATE: Rails of Europe is such a clear-cut improvement over this that it may obsolete it for me. I'll play one more time, but I think my rating is headed for a 6. There are pretty obvious flaws in this game, and they become even harder to overlook when another edition fixes them. 

Update: I've cooled off on Rails of Europe, which fixes a lot of the problems in RRT. Ultimatley, this game has a bad map, bad secret objectives, dull core gameplay (send colored cubes to cities of the matching color), and dumb plastic pieces that are supposed to increase the theme but have no relevance to the actual gameplay. I'd be much more excited about individualized goods. At least I'd feel like I were delivering something. This is an unpolished, generic, ultimately shallow game with a lot of glaring defects and almost nothing to redeem it.

RATING: 6
As someone who struggled in his first couple of games of Age of Steam I can appreciate the accessibility and forgiving nature of Railroad Tycoon. It is better for heterogeneous groups with players of varying levels of experience with a game. It is not much fun in any game to think that you have lost in the first half hour of a three hour game and RRT avoids this.

It is also a pleasure playing on such a beautiful board (if you have the space). The problem with the board size is that it is hard to get a sense of the board because it is all so spread out. This affects gameplay. I pay little attention to anything going on over the main mountain ridge because it is so far from my sight. It doesn't matter too much though because there seems to be little interaction between the east and the midwest.

I am also concerned about the pacing of the game. The games I have played so far have both ended just as players have set themselves up for strong turns. Perhaps it is ending too quickly to allow for larger empire strategies to come to fruition.

The only relationship I can see with the computer game is the name and theme. Just about any of the hundreds of board games with trains are equally suited for the license.

RATING: 7
Based on 4 plays.  A strong game that suffers in the shadow of its older brother.  As for my opinion on the great RRT vs. AoS debate, I come down on the side of AoS.  RRT has a lot going for it: the modified income table is interesting and the flexibility in actions (build/ship/upgrade/etc.) adds a strategic dimension.  However, AoS wins out with a far superior auction, a tighter economy, no goofy cards, and better components (i.e. a board I can fit on my table).  While I wouldn't mind picking up RRT to play with novices, I would almost always prefer AoS.

RATING: 7
[b]Purchase/Selling History[/b]: 
Bought from funagaingames.com fall '07 with CotE and BFiNY
Sold to Millenium with several other games Nov. '16 with the intention of eventually buying Railways of the World.

[b]Rating: 7.0 [/b]
[i]Design rating[/i]: [b]3[/b]
[i]Component rating[/i]: [b].5[/b]
[i]Willingness to play rating[/i]: [b]1.5[/b]
[i]Fun rating[/i]: [b]2[/b]

[b]Players considerations:[/b] More the better, kind of lame with 2-3 even. Not enough balance or tightness to map with that few players.

[b]My thoughts:[/b]
Wow, simply a beautiful game, to look at and play. Strong mechanics and forces players to be very strategic so that other players don't catch on to what move you plan on making.
Fabulous game that is cutthroat and demands good use of economics.
Don't get me wrong, the map is beautiful, but errors in the construction of the hexes and warping is lame. Everything else is great!
Excellent map and bits, not too in depth though it seems, and getting behind is a death sentence when its late in the game and you're unable to create or locate opportunities for massive amounts of points? I haven't played this enough.

RATING: N/A
Railroad Tycoon
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The new and improved edition of Railroad Tycoon. 

A fantastic strategy game that is both challenging and rewarding. It is challenging because, despite simple rules, it requires solid long term planning, contingency planning, investment management, anticipation of opponents actions, adjusting on the fly, opportunism, and sometimes a tiny bit of luck. It is rewarding for these same reasons. Just doing well in this game is quite satisfying regardless of how many times you’ve played or even who wins. 

This is an excellent game and the random distribution of cubes and random selection of cards at the start ensures that every game is different. However it is the selection of expansion maps available, each with their own character and challenges, that really propel this game to infinite replay value and all time favorite. 
 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
While the board layout and warping can be a slight turn off, the game has grown on me and the game far outshines the minor problems. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I like the concept of getting money by taking out loans that cannot ever be repaid, and that hurt you every single turn for the rest of the game.  Immediate agonizing decisions.

The only fix needed is that turn order should be completely bid upon.  It is a massive advantage to be going second after somebody who consistently bids for first.

RATING: 7
A good pickup and deliver rail game that is relatively easy to teach and learn for gamer and non-gamers. It is important to understand the VP/income levels and how best to manage that. The cards can be a little too "lucky" as some are much more powerful than others so I typically play with a variant to help reduce (slightly) the impact. Auction variant can also help with turn order determination.  Rails of Europe is a good 4 person map and has a few other elements that I have brought over to the base map to streamline play.

Rails of Europe

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I played this after having played Age of Steam. Some call it "Age of Steam Lite", which is correct, in a manner of speaking. It's the same 'engine', if you will, but without any of the tension to keep it interesting. The auction, one of Age of Steam's most interesting features, is now hideously boring because there is rarely any difference in what you can do whether you are first or last to play in any given round. The board is FAR too big to support any real tension, and there are certain areas that are virtually worthless.
Plus, an overproduced game should be a feast for your eyes. Railroad Tycoon just looks gaudy.
I highly doubt I will ever play this game again. A rating of 4 is generous for a game that has been so bastardized, but it is saved a lower space on the scale for being one of Eagle's 'better' games. As though that was saying much. Verdict: [b]4[/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is currently my favorite game.

RRT is fairly light yet it manages to give each player interesting decisions. The game can be confrontational with track placement sometimes being fairly tactical (especially in the North-East). 

The randomization of the goods cubes and the operations cards provide for good replayability as well. This sort of randomization is an element in games I particularly enjoy.

This is the game that made me realize that games dealing with finance can be and are fun! I'd love to play some heavier finance/economics games and of course get in to some more train stuff (AoS and 18xx). 

The weakest part of the game for me is the auction, which sometimes just doesn't seem that important. I wish players who went say 2nd or 3rd would also have to pay (a lesser amount though). As it stands simply being to the left of the highest bidder in a turn is/can be an advantage - you get to go second and you didn't have to pay anything. I suppose that's part of the strategy, eh? Seems a little hollow to me though - particularly when you sit to the left of auction winner but aren't building in the same area at all as he is. Oh well. Still this doesn't really detract much from the game for me.

RATING: 8.5
I like the variable setup. I like the strategic implications you can pick out at the start of the game, but how there is enough interaction and randomness to keep things interesting. I like the big easy to read boards. I like that is a longish game that doesn't have any moments of tiredness - I always want it to go longer when I see the end coming. I like that it allows enough space (between turns, or just mentally) to socialise as well as play.

I don't find the things stripped out of Steam and AoS lose much from the game for me. They are meaner, and I like that, but I also find them less thematically engaged, fiddly, and offering less per time invested. I also don't think they differentiate skill differences as much as the extra overhead justifies.

All things being equal, I'd rather play an 18xx when possible, but when it's not possible, I enjoy playing this.

In RR Tycoon, there is a potential runaway leader issue but I do like the card aspects even though it offers a heavier dose of randomness which I don't get from AoS.  The major reason this didn't go well with me is simply that the board is NOT balanced (the northeast is crap).

Update (June2013):  I've now played "Railways of the Eastern U.S." and it's okay.  I really do like the rule change that: your first link of delivery must be your own link.  That's significantly less punishing than the original rule from Age of Steam where anyone can take any goods to any city over any link (even not their own).

RATING: 6
Ez egy könnyen tanulható okos játék HATALMAS játéktáblával. A táblán felül minden játékosnak szüksége van még egy kis helyre, ahol a részvényeit/pénzét/bábujait/kártyalapjait tartja, szóval ez a játék NAGYON helyigényes (pl. a szintén helyigényes Agricolához képest is).

A nagy játéktábla hátránya a helyigényen felül még az is, hogy a játékosok a tábla túlsó végében lévő városokat és pontos útvonalakat nem is látják jól át, sem az éppen "piacon lévő" kártyákat (legalábbis a lapok szövegét messziről biztos nem lehet elolvasni). A játék minimális nyelvismeretet igényel, egyedül az imént említett néhány kártyán van írott szöveg, de azok közül is sokat elég egyszer elolvasni, utána csak a kártyához kapcsolódó városnevet kell nézni, pl. Szállodák, távolsági vasútvonalak két végpontja, stb.

A játék elég intuitív, könnyen megtanulható, viszonylag keveset kell várni a többi játékosra, az újrajátszhatósága sem tűnik rossznak, sőt! A játékidő kb. 2 óra, de elég folyamatosan megy, nem érződik soknak.

Érdemes a játékosoknak a többi játékos játékát is figyelni, az ő céljaikat is figyelembe véve lépni, nekik lehetőleg keresztbe tenni. Okos játéknak érzem, amiben fontos a stratégia, de a taktika is. Apróság, de jó ötlet, hogy minden játékosnak van egy rejtett (csak általa ismert) egyéni célja is, amit ha teljesít, a játék végén plusz pontokat kap (pl. a legkevesebb részvényt kibocsátani, a legtöbb összeköttetést felépíteni a városok között, stb.)

A játékot egy tapasztalt és egy kezdő játékos közül valószínűleg a tapasztalt fogja megnyerni, azaz lehet benne fejlődni, szépen felfedezni, hogy mit hogyan érdemes csinálni, meg lehet tanulni eredményesen játszani.

RATING: 9
Having played Age of Steam, it's difficult for me to judge this game without reference to its predecessor.  I feel that RRT is a very fun game that is nearly as good as Age of Steam itself. The secret objective cards and the operations cards both add a significant bit of chaos but also give the players other goals to consider when building.  I find they add somewhat to the game.  I also like the more lenient monetary system (no bankruptcies), the increased emphasis on theme and the superior components (despite the warped boards).  I find the inability to ship a good along someone else's track for the first leg of its journey flattens the tactical choices a bit and I also prefer the role selection mechanism of the original.

This game makes me want the classic wooden peaces back. Gameplay is intense and most of the time you have to make cruel decisions. There might be a runaway leader if others fall back too far behind. The long playing time (30 minutes per person) and the monstrous board is a big disadvantage. Is there anybody who ever built the Western Link at a profit?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a revised edition of Railroad Tycoon, and the revisions are all improvements. The colours of the cubes better match the spaces on the game board, and although colour-blind players are still pretty much screwed, at least now normal-sighted people can tell the colours apart.

The most significant change is in the rules; all of the major routes are available from the beginning of the game, so that players can plan accordingly without gambling on a lucky card draw.

I think this is a great game. The maps feel genuinely big, which is not the case in Steam (which is a somewhat deeper game). Even a last-place player will feel he has accomplished something.

The components are all of a decent quality, but (and I'm revealing some bias here) you can tell it's not a product of one of the classier European publishers. The artwork is functional, but you'd never describe it as pretty or elegant. Train buffs and 18xxers won't mind this a bit though, but of course, for them, there's far more advanced stuff out there.

Nevertheless, this is great stuff, and I'd love to get it to the table more often.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Eagle Games multi-lingual edition

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A huge board. That is the most important thing to remember about this game. For the rest it's ok, but not great. It takes too long for what it is, and because of the huge board, you are tempted to focus only on the part where you are operating, ignoring the other players that are operating on the other end of the board. 

The importance of the randomly opened (green dot) cards is a bit too high. Once you are behind, its almost impossible to catch up. For a game that last for more than 2 hours, that can be pretty annoying for the person that gets behind.

This game is decided during the first few moves. If you make a mistake there, you can forget it.

Minus: 3 (duration) - 5 - 6 - 8 (unbalanced cards too important, once you get behind, there is no turning back) - [-1]

RATING: 7
Got to play this at euroquest. 
The game is a light version of Age of Steam. If you know that game you can pick up on this one very quickly.

I have a few concerns about this game.

first is the size, its huge! I mean, "some people don't have tables this big" huge.  This size is a problem in that you can't really see whats happenig over on the otherside of the board without moving over there. 

The game play has a bit more luck in it than AOS, but not too much. There are cards that can be selected that may give a few points but a good rail system will win in the end. 

I must admit that the Graphic are nice, however I was very disappointed in the quality of the game and some choices in the board layout. This was a suprise for me since thats usually the only thing Eagle does well. The card stock was rather thin for all of the cards except the tain level cards, and this should be kept on a chart. While the board didnt has as much wasted space as some older Eagle game ther was still more than needed. The score track was tiny and could not be read from the other side of the board; and the trains markers do not fit on there. (colored disks like AoS uses would be better)  The train level cards could have been done better and easier with a chart kept on the board.

The biggest gripe however is with the quality of the board itself. I watched out gameboard warp itself from not so flat to almost scroll like as the game progressed. It became impossible to lay track over a gap by the end of the game. This is very poor and something I have not seen with Eagle games before. I hope Eagle adresses this issue. 

I had purchased a copy at Euro-quest and before we finished out 1st game (with a friends copy) I sold the game to another player.

RATING: 10
[b]11/20/2005 Rating: 8.5[/b] 
Rating from initial playing. This is very cool. Simple rules, lots of strategy, many roads (tracks?) to victory. The money/stock mechanic is brilliant. 

Railroad Tycoon can be taught in just a few minutes and is very straightforward and intuitive to learn. Having the Action summary on the board is nice until you get the hang of what is going on. 

Strategy and tactics are wide and varied as you have so many different areas to work with on your turn. I'm wondering how viable it is to just go off and build up an area on your own as opposed to trying to horn in on areas where otheres are building - looks like I need to play again!

This game LOOKS great all set up. It has a great Euro feel to it with the use of the wooden cubes yet also looks distinctly American with the miniature trains and RR buildings.

I definately want to get this one and play it a bunch to see how much higher my rating will go as well as see what the staying power of it is.

[b]UPDATE 12-26-05 Rating: 9[/b]
Having played this at least 5 times now, I have raised this up to a 9. It may go even higher as time goes by. RRT is a great game with lots of strategy, tactics and - most importantly, FUN. Everyone who has tried this has really enjoyed it and it's been on heavy rotation on our regular Friday game night.

I gave a copy away as a Christmas gift and also got back an enthusiastic response. My copy should be broken in over New Year's... we'll see how it goes.

[b]UPDATE 01-28-06 Rating: 9.5[/b]
Played again last night with 4. Everyone had played many times before and the game was very tense, exciting and fun. The scores were very close (up until the last few rounds). We aren't that cutthroat when playing and I really think playing that way hurts you as much as the other player. We LET someone get the Major Line to Kansas City (with Western Link) and he came in 4th by a wide margin. RRT just keeps getting better and better.

[b]UPDATE 08-14-06 Rating: 10[/b]
This game deserves to be a 10, no questions about it.

[b]UPDATE 08-04-07 Rating: 10[/b]
For me, Railroad Tycoon clearly meets the BBG criteria for a "10" rated game: Outstanding, always want to play and expect this will never change. Played last night and added in my RRT Event Deck - which was the first time my family had ever used them and they went over very well. I'm really looking forward to the "Unofficial Sequel" coming out later this year.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Top-notch all around: the components are like Christmas morning; the base game (second edition) includes a great two-player map on top of the enormous Eastern U.S. map; it's easy to learn and easy to teach; and most importantly it's fun to play.

I really dig the bond mechanic; it seems crippling and painful early on, but once you pass the tipping point your bonds are pretty much forgotten, which is fun. I also love the economy of the turns: There's plenty of room for strategy, both long-term and medium-term, and tactics also plays a role -- but with only three actions every turn, you don't have to spend a lot of time taking your turn, or waiting for others to take theirs.

And best of all, Alysia and I both loved it on our first play. What a fantastic game!

RATING: 8
Played 5 time.
Last Played Feb 24, 2008

We played it just as a two player to learn it before teaching it to friends.  It was a very interesting game.  I made a lot of stupid mistakes so my husband just bearly beat me.  I imagine it will be a much more cut-throat type of game with more players.

Played it again, this time teaching two friends.  I enjoyed the game up until the end when my husband decided to be a jerk.

The fist two times we had been playing it slightly wrong.  From the directions I thought that each tile was considered a link, so with the 8 train you could only go 8 tiles.  But this didn't seem to make sense as it would be impossible to get any of the red cubes from the second western link to Chicago.  So we scoured the directions until we learned what a link really was.  That made the game play quite a bit differently and was definitly a better game.

RATING: 7.6
One of the first games I bought, and one that makes it to the table frequently. The Eastern US map is pretty poorly balanced with fewer than 4 players but really starts to shine with more players. Buying the Western map is a must if you want to play the game with 3-4 people regularly.

You can mix a variety of players regarding skill level and everyone will have fun, but the game does have a "break point" where the more skilled players will have a better grasp on how to win and it will be a mystery to the newer ones.

Overall, this is a game I'm happy to have in my collection.

RATING: 7.5
Very enjoyable train game. More of a train feeling than Age of Steam with its abstract mechanics and calculations. This is easier, but more correct themewise. Offer stocks when you need the money, just as in the real world. In AoS you're offering stocks when you think you will need some money without knowing yet how much. This weirdness creates all the problems of AoS (too much calculations, very unforgiving etc.). These problems are solved here. 
RT however, has a slight problem of having somewhat solitary play at times when there is nobody around you. It also needs to be played with 6 players always to solve this. Another and more serious problem of the game is that the gameboard offers inequal chances. The northeast wins when someone is there alone. If there two there, then the southeast wins. Too many cubes and bonuscards in that area. Nevertheless, still an enjoyable game.

RATING: 8
Game in a Nutshell: Lay track from city to city in order to ship goods from cities that have them to cities that want them. Problem is you have no money and crappy trains, so you'll need to decide how much debt you'll want to deal with for the rest of the game.

Played as: 4 player

Pros: This is the perfect blend of economic, tile-laying, and transportation I imagine is possible. The money is simple but heavily considered and not so much in the 'mathy' sort of way but simply as a 'do I need more right now or am I ok?' The open information of the board makes the decisions fair in where to lay their track and is balanced by bidding on who gets to lay down on those sweet spots first.

Cons: It also has one of the biggest downfalls a game could have: It's easy to know who's going to win after 15 minutes if a person gets a good start and from there you only have to play another 2 hours of pointless non-climactic gaming. There's no good way of changing direction mid-stream to adjust to this eather. At less then five players, you eather guarantee this happening or run too good of a chance that it's borderline not worth playing. Furthermore, it's not a game that's going to have a lot of casual 'Cuz, it doesn't make you think as much,' Ticket to Ride gamers waiting to play, because it's really a more gamers game then a social one. So good luck having enough 'gamers' around to make it worth it.

Other: I was really impressed with this game after the first play for all the reasons stated in my 'pros' and really disappointed after the second run for all the listed 'cons.' I really want to consider this one of my favorite games and I still have hope due to there being a chance of more competition with more players. Also, I've yet to play the full compliment of rules with the cards / western link and hopefully that'll bring the game in and make it more interesting as well.

Best Use: A good gamers choice when you like the openness of tile-laying (like me) but want to mix it up with some light economics and trains for a couple hours.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Received in Trade.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I can't decide whether this is better or worse than Steam or Age of Steam.  Steam, at an abstract aesthetic level, seems to have a better sense of proportion, and to integrate the theme better with a minimum of chrome.  The board is much more compact and functional than RRT's.  Steam relies more heavily on the bidding mechanic, which makes it unfriendly to newcomers.

Railroad tycoon, on the other hand, is more sprawling, and has maybe too many cards, and a couple broken ones.  But you can focus more on building than bidding, which I like. There are late events, like the western link, that make the game livelier at the end, where Steam can wind down early as the cubes disappear.  Also, the cards can lead to some creative play.  

Railroad tycoon works quite well as a two-player game, and for that number I like it much better than the Steam expansions.

RATING: 9.5
One of our group's favorites for multiplayer, and almost certainly my most played game.  It's simple, plays reasonably fast (2-3 hours), doesn't have much in the way of random elements, doesn't have that pesky feature of not being able to perform an action because someone else took that action for this turn, has no real gang-up possibilities common in multiplayer games, and has numerous expansion maps.  The rules are short, but gameplay requires a good amount of thought/planning to play effectively.  It's deep enough that it requires a lot of concentration, but not so much so that you can't BS in between turns just a little bit. 

We do play with a few simple house rules that bump this game to 'perfect' status.

RATING: 8
Another excellent game from Mr Wallace.  It's very tempting to compare with AoS but this is altogether a different game for a different playing environment/group, and in my opinion is in no way less enjoyable.  

The components, accessibility and added tactical dimension offered by the cards make this a game of balance.  Can you take advantage of any quick tactical wins offered by the cards yet still attain the longer term objectives offered by longer shipping routes, your Tycoon objectives and more strategic Operations cards?

For some variety make up Chris Boote's excellent European map and cards variant.

RATING: 8.9
Acquired March 31st, 2008.  

The last bunch of games I bought was in January, so I figured I would treat myself.  I was debating on getting a fewer less expensive games or this.....I chose the latter.  The reason I picked this up is our gaming group recently played Volldampf, which I quite enjoyed.  Yes the price tag of this game is steap, but worth every penny in my book.  I have the internation verision (2nd edition?), which means the engine level cards, the board, and the shares are all written in spanish. (Except on the board it does say Railroad Tycoon in English, as well as all the cities.)  I can live with the spanish, but not the 1st edition's major color problems between blue and purple, but more importantly the board warping issues was a major turn-off.  Plus Eagle games so kindly included player aids in the 2nd edition!
I have not tried or read up on AoS, but this game is very similar to Volldampf, except for the stock/share.  In RRT, you borrow anytime, in Volldampf, you must decide  how much you want to borrow during one of the phases so you can lay your track in a later phase.
In RRT this board is huge!!!  36" x 45"  I feel it takes Volldampf one step further, but actually like Volldampf a bit more.  (Plus one of our gamming members in our group owns Volldampf, so if we want to play it, does not make sence for me to acquire one as well, plus it is a rare game and I wanted something different).  In RRT you need to upgrade your engines, pay for track accordingly to the terrian it crosses, ship goods, build cities, or pick a special action card.
All the components are excellent.
The cubes (goods) which are in different locations every game make each gamming experience unique.  My only downfall(s) of this game would be that even if though the game board is huge, it does get slightly busy because of the tracks being laid and the competion for the NorthEast (which brings debate to if this game is broken).  The scoring track is also on the small side, and will not fit multiple trains.  But please, don't let any of this detract from buying.  These are all minor compaired to how great I feel this game truley is.  Once the game is complete, all the components in their final palce is very visually appealing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like Railroad Tycoon. It certainly is a notch or three down from Age of Steam in "thinkiness" but that doesn't mean it still doesn't have a lot of interesting decisions each game. Like other classic Euros... there is always that feeling that you'd like to do 3 different things but you can only do one thing at a time so there is always an optimization going on and reevaluating where your actions are best spent. I've only played this 3 player so far and yes there is a LOT of room out there and I can certainly see where it would be better with 4-6 players but I still like the game quite a lot. With more players I'm almost certain my rating would rise as their would be more conflict and more confrontational areas on the gameboard. I found the rules to be rather easy to grok.. there were a few niggly bits in the rules but easy enough to straighten out with the use of faqs and from answers in the rules forum on BGG.

Areas of the game that could use improvement... well I like the big board which others seem to dislike but it would have been nice to have the reverse of the board printed with 2 player or 3 players maps, say 1 board panel for 2 players and 2 board panels for 3 players... just to make a more compact and closed-in area to encourage more confrontation.

Other than that I think the game is solid. If some don't like the Tycoon cards because they think they are unbalancing then chuck them out and don't use them... same goes for the major lines cards... which can be points falling in your lap when they show up after someone has already completed a link. They can likely be chucked as well... or simply select a few and play those face up from the start of the game... everyone then has an equal shot at them all... no surprises.

The auction is a bit screwy and many hate it. I don't think it's a game breaker but it can also be altered as a house rule as well. The game is actually quite adaptable and with user generated maps for more variety there is extra life to the game as well.

I wouldn't say the game is better or worse than Age of Steam... just different. It's certainly easier to get RT on the table with a wider range of gamers than AoS so for that alone it wins points with me. Game time when we first played was something like 90 minutes (probably closer to 2 hours with setup and rules explanations) but I can see this consistently being played in 1.5 hours or so once everyone is familiar with the gameplay. Play length is almost perfect actually as you don't get bored of the gameplay and it leaves you with a feeling of "if only I had a few more rounds to do x and y". A good sign as many games start feeling "samey" near their conclusions.

A solid 8 game for me right now with a good chance at moving up once I play with 4 or 5.

RATING: 6.5
Wow, a neat game. I admit it, I'm a sucker for bits. But I think the game itself is pretty good, too. I haven't played Age of Steam yet, and I'm sure that's a better system, but I'm having lots of fun with this. 

Update: Downgraded the game a couple points. I've played it about 15 times now with (with all numbers of players, but mostly with 5-6) And while I find the basic mechnics good, I'm now starting to get annoyed by a few things: the random card draws (seem to have a huge impact on the game flow), the auction (who you're sitting next to is huge, so huge I've actually considered changing seats in my game), the problem with the southwest part of the map (it never gets used and therefore it might as well not even be there), and the tycoon cards don't seem balanced (particulary if you've drawn one of the doubles that forces you into a competition that the other players don't have to worry about). All in all, I certainly don't think RRT is a bad game, and I have had lots of fun, but I'm just finding it harder to get into it given these issues (All of which seem to be addressed in AoS--which I've finally played--but sadly, my group shows little interest in the original.)

RATING: 9
One of my favorites. Easy to teach and learn, plays fast, short setup time, nice production. There's hardly any downtime, and plays pretty fast.

Downside to this in comparison to Rails over Europe (and why this isn't a 10), is the unnecessary large map. The map favors the northeast heavily. Routes are represented by cards which are not visible to people on the other end of the huge map. Interaction is lower, and Western Link feature sucks.

All in all, great game, but get Rails over Europe in addition to this!!

RATING: 7.5
The updated version of Age of Steam. The main differences are that in a turn, there are 3 rounds, and everyone gets to execute any action of their choice (urbanize, increase links, deliver goods). There are also cards that give VPs for achieving a certain goal, such as connecting cities. There are also secret condition VPs as well. This is a nice component game, more forgiving than AoS, and fun to play. I'm sure this game will generate countless debate about which game is better to play.

I prefer this to Age of Steam...not because it's bigger, newer, and prettier, but because it's less tightly focused, it works (surprisingly well) with only two players, and your score doesn't decline with your income.  It certainly isn't perfect, though.  There can be major runaway leader problems, there are two identical pairs of Tycoon bonus cards (which means that if two players get dealt the same one, at least one of them can't possibly get the bonus...unfair!), and the fact that bidding is only for going first means that being seated to the left or right of the most aggressive bidder can make or break you.  (This is one aspect in which Age of Steam is clearly the better game;  no constantly going second for free!)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

My favorite train game. I love the components! It's easy to learn and very interactive. You can block other players. You just need a big table!

RATING: 8.5
Initial impression was very favourable - the "Droverisation" of Age of Steam appears to have gone off without a hitch.  Others have already listed the various changes between this and AoS, but I will point out four tweaks that I feel make the game more user-friendly and less cutthroat (not to mention bringing the learning curve down to a more 'casual' gamer level):
1) All initial goods cubes start the game on the board.  The AoS mechanic of rolling for production always bugged me, as it meant that you could get hosed by die rolls in what is otherwise a very deep 'gamer' game.  In RT, the board is much easier to survey and the 'good' spots to start the game are more easily spotted.
2)Shares can be issued at any time.  This is very nice, since it removes the start-of-round math session from AoS where all players try and calculate what they'll need this turn - and woe to them if they calculate wrong or get blocked by someone else.  In RT, you issue 'em when you need 'em - much easier to handle for players not used to having to plan far in advance.
3) Income track starts at 3.  A seemingly minor change, it means that a player issuing 2 shares at the start of the game only needs to complete a single 1-link delivery to be above break-even point.  Compare this to the AoS model where you have 2 shares to start as well as upkeep on your engine meaning that you need to ship 3 cubes before you can even tread water - clearly, AoS is more punishing in its financial model.  A major turn-off for non-hardcore players.
4) All goods may only be shipped along links where the first link is owned by the shipping player.  Again, a seemingly minor tweak, but it makes the game a lot less cutthroat since it's now harder for others to ship cubes out from under your nose.  This means that you can plan ahead in comfort and usually be sure that your plans won't be completely undone by the time your turn hits.  In AoS, I always found there to be more downtime as players scrambled to adjust to the board position that the previous player left them.  For all its size and sweeping scope, RT moves along at a surprisingly decent clip.

I will still play AoS, but only with a certain group.  RT will likely be the game of choice whenever a lighter, more engaging version of the game is called for - and as long as the game holds up to my initial impressions, I can see that being the case more often than not.

Update: Have now played 3 times.  My only concern thus far is the occasional 8-10 point "Major Line" card coming up when it's nearly a gimme for one player.  Perhaps cut the value in half in those situations?  Hardly a major quibble and I've actually raised my rating by half a point as of this update.

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*方塊放袋中，隨機抽補滿每個城市上顆數，2/3人每個城市少1(最少1顆)，用一個空城市標記記錄回合
*起始用競標的，每人拿一個顏色火車，1個1號火車頭
*起始沒有錢，隨時缺錢就拿債卷，一個債卷值5-000，債卷不能還，最後每個扣1分
*遊戲每輪分三個階段，直到達到特定數目的空城市遊戲結束
1.競標起始 右上輪起始起標(至少1-000)，最高價得標，之後順時鐘，全pass則傳給上輪起始的左邊玩家
2.每人輪流行動共三次，可選行動如下
>建鐵路 建鐵軌連結城市(最多四個如果沒連到，連到就停)，所有沒有連到的鐵路在此大輪結束後移除，有連到的上面放你的火車頭，每個鐵軌根據位置付對應金額
>都市化 付10-000，選一個一旁的城市放在地圖灰色城市上，抽兩個貨物放上面，原本上面貨物還是在上面，如果有空城市標記移除
>升級火車頭 附升級火車頭上面的錢升一級，換對應火車頭版塊，最高8
>運貨 移動貨物最多經過火車頭等級的城市，目標城市要跟貨物同色，不能重複經過城市，經過該色就要停，每個有經過的鐵軌玩家獲得1分(第一段鐵軌一定要是自己的)，貨物被運光就放空城市標記
>拿鐵路運作卡 綠色完成條件就拿並移除(不花動作)，紫色每回用可用一次，紅X拿了馬上用完棄掉，黑卡放手上，想用在出，出卡不算行動
*起始把有S的卡先開著放，在開人數*2張卡，每回合結束在開一張
3.收入跟分紅 根據收入軌拿收入，之後根據債卷每股付1-000，輪次標記回到1，沒完成連結鐵軌棄掉
*遊戲最後比收入軌分數，平手比地圖上鐵軌連結束，之後在比現金

****美西****
*每人起始隨機兩張大亨卡，每人暗選一張，遊戲最後打開拿分數
*有點的是山脈(即使上面有水)
*主幹線運作卡一開始就全開(可選擇不要，照洗進牌庫裡)
*只有缺錢才能拿債卷
*有連結到kansas/desmonines的人有額外行動可作，可花30-000做特殊連結，放對應板塊並從袋中抽4顆紅色貨物放到該城市，當有特殊連結後，如果此紅色貨物運到chicago，則chicaga抽兩個隨機貨物
*遊戲結束條件 2/3/4/5/6人時，10/12/14/16/18空城市

**墨西哥***
*起始每個城市隨機抽貨物(2人時少1，最少1)，最年輕起始，新城市版塊不用
*遊戲結束條件 2/3/4人時，7/8/9空城市，平手比錢

RATING: 6.5
A good solid middle weight game. There's lots of setup, but it does look good, although there are a few minor production issues (colour, warp, low quality cards) but they don't harm the game too much.

The game design is a bit rough around the edges in terms of luck of the draw (initial Tycoon cards, major lines), the turn order auction (2nd player gets a big free advantage), and the way the map is highly asymmetric. RRT:Europe addresses most of these issues, as does the reprint "Railways of the World"

RATING: N/A
1500 已拆未玩
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've come to prefer the original Railroad Tycoon over Age Of Steam, liking the variety and the luck of the cards. The imbalance of the American map is unfortunate because approaches don't vary much between games. The European map generates more competition, more route variety and an overall tenser affair. The auction is a touch too simplistic and unfair on the new last player, but there's much to like in the game. The challenge and aim is to identify and develop 4, 5 and 6 length routes and execute on them before other people steal the cubes, whilst needing to get small 1 and 2 length routes executed quickly so that your net balance (income over share cost) is ahead as quickly as possible. Therefore plenty to think about and plenty of competition to make life challenging.
The Mexico map doesn't provide anything much new or special, just map variety for 3 or 4 players. It encourages players to progress around the edges to get the city-to-city building bonus points though, and there's not a lot in the middle to entice you in anyways. Our one 3 player game saw a lot of independent track building / running until we were up to 5 or so links and then touching on each other's cities. There'll obviously be more congestion with 4 players though, so play with the number that suits the type of game you prefer.
