=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Quite the enjoyable train game. It's nicely produced and quite pretty. The maps are HUGE. It's a bit too much luck driven to be as fun as I'd like it to be. My biggest annoyance with the game is the severe lack of track pieces in things other than gentle curve and straight. Sometimes you really need a hard curve. My other gripe with the game is the cards can be really swingy in a bad way. Getting 4+ points in addition to what you just did can really add up quickly.

I still vastly prefer Age of Steam/Steam to this game, but it's a much "nicer" experience and plays well with most gamer types. This game is surprisingly tough at times though and you can really get into problems with the amount of bonds/income you get. Or at least the Mexico map can be a bit brutal with 2 if all the goods cubes are in horrible places.

I enjoy all the maps so far, but England/Wales has to be my favorite, followed by Europe, Mexico and the Time Maps. I don't really care for the main 6 player map that the base comes with. It's far too broken to be fun.

One additional complaint that's surfacing the more that I play it. Money is so loose in this game most of the time. We played a 3 player map with 2 players and we had almost ~$100 a piece at one point. We had 8 total loans between the 2 of us. This game is crazy loose. Fun, but man do I miss the tension in Steam/Age of Steam.

Variants used: 

[b]AOS bidding system[/b]: Hate the rules as written for this game. 2nd player has too much of an advantage for not paying a damn thing. If you bid, you're likely to pay something.

[b]Track redirect[/b]: Sometimes you get screwed over and need to redirect your track. 2k.

[b]Building over previous track[/b]: 3k always. Makes it much simpler.

[b]Major lines available at all times[/b]

RATING: 9
Wow, this game has a really big board.... it cracks me up every time I read a review or session report for RRT because they almost always start with this. Of course, I opened the box for the first time and said "Wow, that's a really big board" myself.

My hubby gives this one a 10.... and it's definitely right up there for me. The mechanic of actually laying down raidroad track tiles pretty much anywhere you'd like makes it so much fun. We've only played two-player, so we've done away with the turn order auction, which I imagine adds a lot of challenge to the game if you have more players.

Updated to add: Finally had a chance to play with more than two. I'm not sure I liked it as much-- it made the game a lot longer and there was a lot of down time. Still, a solid game, however!

This would definitely get more play with a smaller board... we both really love this game.

RATING: 6
A slightly heavier train game than Ticket to Ride that is still easy enough to teach new players. This is a decent euro game that could still use some stream lining. I have never been a fan of wonky scoring systems but I will play this. What is inexcusable is that Eagle-Gryphon Games does not publish the rules to this online in PDF format.

Pros:
* Good quality components.
* Huge game board.

Cons:
* [b]-1 for Eagle-Gryphon Games not publishing the rules online in PDF format. Inexcusable.[/b]
* You can easily run out of table space to play this game as the game board may actually be too big.
* Wonky score tracking board and scoring system.
* I am not a big fan of the perpetual debt system and the corresponding negative points.
* You can easily get in a debt spiral that is impossible to recover from.
* The additional Mexico map is horrible.

RATING: 8
I came at this after several plays of Age of Steam.  Both have very similar systems, but Tycoon has a lot more bells and whistles.  AoS has the harsher economic system, but Tycoon will punish you, too.  Knowing the various Tycoon bonuses and what the cards do is kind of a pain in the first play of Tycoon, and the map seems to have more options, but I'm not sure that it does, as you're doing 3x as many things per turn.  It's primary advantage is being pretty enough and simple enough for less hard core players, and I was thrown by a couple subtle rule changes from AoS to Tycoon, including the way the game ends (the turn AFTER someone triggers the max. empty city # - in AoS it's a set # of turns).

RATING: 8.5
Train game that feels somewhat similar to the crayon rails games but without the actual train movement. Instead planning/building routes and deliveries is the focus here, with additional auction & income elements that require you to manage your spending in a similar way to Power Grid.

Plays ok with 2 but the map is definitely too large, so there is little competition and a very abrupt end to the game (perhaps playing to more than the prescribed number of empty cities would help?). The Rails of Europe expansion map is probably better for 2 players.

Excellent components although the box is unusually large, and the board is quite possibly the biggest I've seen (only Arkham Horror may come close).

OWN: International Edition which consists of mainly English-language components with a few non-English parts (board and some unimportant contents of cards).

NOTE: New edition planned (2009?) will be a new base game with a paper map of Mexico instead of the original map of eastern USA (which will be a separate expansion).

Of the three near-identical Wallace railroad games,this is his most thematic. I like the larger board, I like the unnecessary plastic bits to mark empty cities, and I love the action cards that consist mostly of men with awesome mustaches. 

Every action takes some thought and planning. I love trying to figure out if it pays to build a railroad around someone else's route or it it's better to ship goods over their route and share in points.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:nostar: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 10
I love this game so much, everything about the design is just so great.  I like that you bid to go first each turn, and I love that you start with no money and immediately have to start managing loans.  Lastly, I love that the game's end condition can be highly controlled by the players themselves.

There's just so much that I really love about this game.  It is fairly long which I really enjoy but it never gets too long and you never feel like you're not doing anything.  The randomness of the goods placement can occasionally make for an unbalanced play, but at the same time I love that you always have to change your plans each time you play.

RATING: 8
This is an excellent variant of Age of Steam, and I prefer Railroad Tycoon to Age of Steam.

(My copy is the original version published as Railroad Tycoon, later editions had a change of name to Railways of the World.)

Railroad Tycoon has very little in common with the excellent Railroad Tycoon 3 computer game. (Although I have played RT3 for many hundreds of enjoyable hours.)

Two expansions, Rails of Europe and Railways of England and Wales, are both excellent expansions for Railroad Tycoon.  However, Railways of England and Wales also includes an optional 'Advanced' game set of rules which is essentially a light version of 18xx, and this version of the game I rate as a 9.

Railroad Tycoon is a great game!  Players build railroads that connect sities and can then deliver goods from one city to another along their railroad network. If you're not careful you can lose this game early on, but that also makes things fun!

RATING: 8
Oh, it could have been a "10".  However, it is the flaws that stand out and drag it down - the board (where blue=purple and the whole thing takes on a gentle curve 30 minutes after opening the box) and the cards (not all Tycoons are created equal, and if your bonus turns up a just the right time ...).

That said, this was one of my favourites of 2005.  OK, so it requires a gargantuan table, but ignore comments that this is "no Age Of Steam".  Indeed not - it's far more approachable and dare I say it fun.  Some people will prefer the cut-throat, hardcore, brain-burning intensity of RRT's older brother, but I like the way that ordinary people can sit down and enjoy a railway game without headaches.  

So long as the light is good enough to tell blue from purple, anyway.   

But I've been seduced away from Railroad Tycoon by a new railway game ...

RATING: 7
Much MUCH more enjoyable than Age of Steam or the 18xx series of railroad games because of many factors. In this one, you concentrate on building a railroad in the most efficient way, competing with other players for scarce resources. Everyone can improve their trains on the SAME turn! Everyone can build track on the SAME turn! You don't have to spend half your time on Wall Street defending your company against raiders! In short, Railroad Tycoon is about the TRAINS!! Hey, that's what a railroad game SHOULD be about. Ok, that said, I'm not sure I'd want to play it every week. It's a rather long game and the only thing you can do while waiting for other players to make their moves is to tell them what they should be doing, which often annoys them. It was fun, and I think playing it every few months or so will keep it that way.

RATING: 9
Updated rating. Yep, this is the best iteration of the system, the best track-laying and delivery game, and simply an all-around great game.

========================================================

Very enjoyable. Handsome components. Doesn't overstay its welcome.

Plays cleaner than Age of Steam or Steam. Does punish early mistakes and inefficiencies - just not as brutally as those games. The railways operations cards and bonus routes are nice enhancements. They give Railways of the World a looseness and variability that the other games lack. 

This looks like it will become our railway game of choice. We often end a game of Age of Steam or Steam with bitterness and recriminations - the cramped computational horizon of those games lead to a sour tone.  That feeling is dispelled in this iteration of the system.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is a game where carefully building a network of railroad links between cities is paramount.  The object of the game is to move goods from one city to a target city (based on the color of the city/good).  Players do this by linking the cities together with railroad tracks.  Players only get 3 actions each turn, thus giving the player some tension in deciding the optimal play for the turn.  For each link traversed in the delivery of a good the player whose link was used gains a point on the income track.  After everyone has taken their three actions there is an income phase.  Players have to issue bonds when they need money, and they pay back each bond each turn, so gaining independent income is rather important.  Players do not start the game with money, so debt is inevitable, but controlling how much debt one incurs is part of the game.

The game has a good depth of strategy, and no two sessions will play out the same since the goods are randomized each game.  There is a good balance between upgrading engines, building tracks, and delivering goods, combined with the income track which increases in value until the midpoint, and then decreases.  The income track doubles as the victory point board, so one does want to increase to win the game, but increasing too fast too soon will deprive you of money.

The game is not too deep as to prevent people from playing it, yet it is by no means light.  A good strategy will be clearly rewarded.  The game is somewhat unforgiving for bad play, but there is room and time to recover and put up a good fight.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent!!  Instant classic -- kinda like a melding of TtR and Power Grid

Woof!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Wow! It's just all so over the top - this one clearly came from the land of bigger is better! I think the bits are grand - except for the confusing colours. They really could have got away with knocking a few cities off the map, made it two thirds the size, still have had the most stunning board and made the game a little more tense, with a little more conflict (and I wouldn't have to spend most of the game standing so I could walk around to different parts of the table). The game should give you the opportunity to play in different ways and the decisions are reasonably interesting. Player interaction is a little low for my taste, but I think I prefer to Age of Steam for the simplicity of a raw game system. The game has the potential to play quickly and the starting cards and cube distribution should see you playing in different ways each outing. Worth every cent really.

Update: The spectacle is still there, but the shine has worn off. Ultimately you're trying to avoid conflict and congestion. There's whinging when players start too close to one another, negative vibes when cubes are whisked away under the nose of other players and often no congratulations to the winning player for a deserved win - it's all put down to them having flown under the radar and not having been contested enough. There's a sour note to this game that's spreading to all it's permutations. And the number of incarnations of this system has gone crazy. Now we have players who prefer one over another, won't touch one over another, blah, blah, blah. I'm sick of pushing little cubes around anyway guys and gals. They're not real trains! It's just a game with a multitude of different maps to try and convince you you're not bored with the whole efficiency/cube pushing exercise.

I'll still play it every once in a while, and if I had to be playing a train game it'd probably be this. If you need a train game in your collection then this is the one. It's the middle ground train game of choice.

RATING: N/A
I wasn't really sure what to expect from Railways of the World. It was the first new game in a while that I hadn't researched much or found out about before actually playing. I knew it was a train game, which didn't particularly excite me.

But this game is great! It's not very heavy at all; the available actions are manageable and easy to understand. But the timing of the actions you take and the board setup is very intriguing. Should I try an claim a route, or should I upgrade my locomotive first? What cards are available, and should I bid for first player? What mission card did I take at the beginning, and should I focus on it? How much debt should I incur?

We played with 5, which I thought worked well on the Western US map, but I don't have much to compare it to. And after only 1 play, it was one of my favorite new-to-me games of 2014.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Maybe you're thinking, "I would love to have a railroad-themed board game, but Ticket to Ride is terrible!" That was me, and bless it, I picked up Railways of the World. First of all, Railways is amazingly thematic. Unlike Ticket to Ride where you are just drawing random colors and putting down trains, in Railways you actually feel like a Railroad Tycoon, building track to deliver valuable goods to where they are demanded. It's so much fun, and it's not difficult to learn (the rule book is only a few pages). If you are interested in 19th Century railroad history, this is a no brainer. Get it. Even if you're not interested (my wife) this game is still a lot of fun. I can't wait to get the Western US expansion!

RATING: 6
[2012-02-25]
A harmadik nagy vonatos játék a sorban, amit ki tudtam próbálni. Steam, Age of Steam és most a Railroad. Gyönyörű hatalmas tábla, könnyedebb játékmenet, TTR-es lapmechanizmus. A legszebb kivitelű játék a három közül és a legkönnyebb is. A lapos mechanizmus nem csak színesíti a játékot, de néhol még segít is a bajbajutott játékosokon. A hitel kezelése is sokkal egyszerűbb. Minden adva van a jósághoz, de a végfeltétel sajnos nem jó. Ha kiürül x darab város, akkor van vége a játéknak. A jól álló játékos ezt könnyűszerrel siettetheti, míg a rosszabbul állók hiába próbálják késleltetni csak még nagyobb hátrányba kerülnek. Ez kétjátékos esetében sajnos nagyon sokat számít, bár egyértelműen nem kétjátékosra készült a társas. Ettől függetlenül szeretném majd még játszani, egyszer talán hatan is. Most egy 6-ossal indul, de emelek a ponton majd ha többen nem okoz gondot ez a végfeltétel dolog.

[2014-02-08]
Sikerült kipróbálni 5-en is. A végfeltétel siettetése most nem annyira jött ki, mert az egyik játékos annyira elhúzott, hogy tulajgondképpen bármikor végetérhetett volna számára nem volt esélyünk behozni. Most ez a dolog jött ki erősen, hogy már az első két kör után kialakult a játékbeli végsorrend. Bár a többi játékos között azért szorosabb lett a dolog, de akkor is látható volt, hogy nagyon büntet a játék. A másik dolog a gyönyörű térkép nem nagyon segít abban, hogy adott hexa pontosan melyik térképtípushoz tartozik, igaz ebben az elején meg lehet közösen állapodni, hogy később ne legyen belőle vita. Egyértelműen ez a játék tetszik az ugyanolyan típusúak közül a legkevésbé, de nem csökkentek a pontján.

RATING: 4
This isn't as bad as I thought it was going to be.  It really does get a bad rap.  There is nothing wrong with this game, but contrary to that there is nothing really all that great to it either.  It is fine if you want to veg out and just move pieces around on autopilot.  This is more like playing a dungeon crawl game (where you just work mindlessly toward leveling up) or a civ building game (where you observe from a distance the "empire" you are building).

That said: 
The turn order auction is either irritating or a waste of time depending on the situation. 
The map is too big to be competitive on. 
There is a ton of wasted space and useless cities. 
The western expansion is silly as it is never worth it to ship two reds, two spaces when you are invariably going to have a three or four train by the time that they are relevant. 
By not scoring points based on what track you build, your strategy is constrained by the cards you draw in the beginning and what may potentially work for you from the draft. The length of the game just isn't warranted by how little you have to think or pay attention to it.  
Decisions are easy; timing is more a key, but even then there isn't much competition so it isn't that much of a concern. 
The economic system is too loosey goosey, there is never a pinch on money. 
Components interfere with the playability as the colors of the cities get covered by the pieces. 
River rules are needlessly confusing (just make the damn hex more expensive) and there isn't the same level of blocking being that it doesn't cost to replace track with complex crossings.

I'm not sure why I would ever play this again over AoS or a quicker 18xx game as it is just as long and not nearly as rewarding.  Of course, this works well enough that I would play it as an option not to play a worse game.

RATING: 6
This is clearly a simplified, streamlined version of Age of Steam. On a freakishly large board. Really, it's freaking huge - and for no good reason! But it's the same railroad-building, cube delivering game. Track has no value other than for delivery (no VP at end). The money is not nearly as tight (can issue stock shares whenever you want; income increases very quickly so you become profitable earlier; you're only auctioning for first place -- since no one else pays, there's more cash in the game ... sit to the left of the big spender if you can!). Also, there is much more randomness because of the cards: tycoon cards give everyone a different goal for bonus points at the end and the operation cards that come up every round can be a real gift sometimes. During the game you build networks of track connecting cities, and deliver cubes over track (hopefully mostly your own) segments to increase your VP. Each VP is associated with a per-turn income, that initially goes up (max income $24K, I think) then goes down (so with 100VP, for example, your income is only $9). Every turn you must pay expenses: price of tracks built, actions taken, and shares previously issued (shares are with you till game end). The game ends when x (depends on number of players) towns/cities have been depleted of their cubes. Then you lose 1VP/share issued. Most VP wins.

Cool bits, but the board is WAY too big (hard to place on table, hard to play on 'cause of difficulty reaching, seemingly lots of room for expansion) and there's too much randomness in the cards. Go with Age of Steam!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is lighter and more "friendly" fare than AoS, and given the choice between the two, I'd go with AoS--luckily I don't have to choose :)  The rules are short, but not particularly clear, so make sure you check out the FAQ before play if you want to get everything as intended. 

There's no doubt the game is GORGEOUS, but it's also GINORMOUS!  It takes up the entirety of our dining table--and really the score track could be put off on a credenza, or side table. But the good part is there's very little that has to sit in front of a player--just their money and loans.  Even their trains could be kept separate, since the number you have left is not really relevant at any time.  The main difficulty comes in trying to see the whole map--nearly impossible while sitting down, you'll probably have to get up often to look for city names and whatnot until you're family with the abstracted geography on the board.

This is a much more forgiving game than AoS, and requires less razor-accurate accounting to play optimally--you can get by much better with non-optimal loans (too few OR too many) and still stay competitive.  This is both good and bad.  On the good side it definitely opens the way for more variation in strategies.  On the bad side, it definitely feels less intense.  The auction mechanic is very watered down, as its entirely just for turn order--which plays a part in AoS, but the auction for role makes AoS far more aggressively competitve.

All in all, I'd recommend it if you have the shelf space for a large box, and the table size for a giant map, but if you lack either and play in a group that can handle the intense competition, AoS will be much more fun.

RATING: 7.5
[1 play] Initial reaction rating.  So of course the natural thing to do is to compare this game with Age of Steam.  Well having played this only once, and AoS twice I really have not made up my mind which is better.  I *think* I might slightly favor AoS but that could easily change if I played the games more.  I do like the nice BIG map in Railroad Tycoon.  In general I am a big fan of railroad games, this one has plenty of good thinking and planning.  I certainly like to play this game again.  The one thing that bugged me about the game was the special cards that gave you points for connecting some cities or doing some delivery.  Maybe I could have focused on them better right at the start when the inital set of them came out, but during the game I didn't get a single one of them and other players got a lot of them.  A new one would come out and say to do something, and some player would be totally setup to do that very thing and poof there is five points for them.  Just seemed like a luck factor I could have done without.  Maybe if I played it more and worked harder to get some of the initial ones, or realized what ones are in the deck and how to setup to get them better I would not mind them as much, I don't know.

RATING: 8
[Eagle Games 2005 edition] {Railroad, economic; set collection, secret-goal, pick-up-and-delivery; optimal 5p} Railroad Tycoon is a good pick-up-and-delivery game.  It is easy to learn and teach.  It is interactive and fun. And, it supports both strategic and tactical options.  It is easier to master than Age of Steam and to control cash flow.  The Railroad operation cards add fun and surprises to the game and they increase the importance of player order bidding.  I own both Age of Steam and Railroad Tycoon.  Both are excellent games.  Personally, I prefer Age of Steam.  But I will pick Railroad Tycoon for general friends.

The board is horribly large, which makes the game only fun and exciting with more people, though it increases downtime.

The components are nicely produced, somewhat over-produced.  However, it would be clumsy to handle the three-part board with a small table.

RATING: 6
User-friendly and looks great, with some chaotic swings via the card additions. The map is unbalanced however, with the Northeast being prime real estate and the Southwest being a vast wasteland.

RAILWAY TYCOON improved by RAILWAYS OF THE WORLD (1st print) which itself is improved by RAILWAYS OF THE WORLD (2nd). With a 3rd printing about to hit the shelves, that's a fine testimony to this engaging game.

It's annoying that there is a simple screw-up or two in each and every release. More attention-to-detail from Eagle would be greatly appreciated in order to avoid these problems.

Overall, this takes a (far) back seat to 18xx and Age of Steam. I don't think our group has any interest in playing this series again.

RATING: 8
Over-produced pick up and deliver railway themed game. The mechanics are drawn from a very strong source, that being 'Age of Steam, where players have to construct tracks running between different towns and cities of differing colours. These settlements contain coloured cubes that have then to be transported to towns/cities that match their colour. The further a cube is transported, the more income a player earns, which is mainly how victory is determined. The production qualities are of the highest order and the game can play quite differently each time depending on the initial placement of the coloured cubes, thereby making this a stand-out game for fans of railway games. There is however a drop-off in strategic depth from the original 'AoS', which leads to it feeling slightly light on meaningful decision making.

RATING: 9
Played 10+ times.  The board is big and pretty.  I like the tension between having all your options available but only being able to do them one at a time. The game play is similar to AoS but more forgiving.  The first player auction is less tense and there is generally only a couple times in the game when going first is important to you. I like how the cards make the game a bit different each time and how they affect the game.  I also like the cube production is less random than Age of Steam.  Conclusion: This game is easier to introduce people to than Age of Steam, but lacks the bite that makes AoS my favorite game. Note: you need to be careful of the NW; lots of points available for cheap. 2008/11/17

RATING: 10
I love this game. It´s my favourite (non – war) strategy game. I love it´s clean design, it´s easy to learn but hard to master rules. The nearly no luck all skill gameplay pared with just the right amount of randomness and swingy situations (cards and random cubes you may try to pull from the back).

The only downside of the game is that I get very mixed responses from the players I play it with. Ranging from: “there is nearly no interaction and it is a very solo-style game” too “I found it too tense and too competitive, I got really upset when someone spoiled my optimisation plan”. So that I have a kind of hard time to bring it to the table and establish a regular group.

RATING: 7
This was my first railroad game, having only played Ticket to Ride, and I enjoyed it.  The whole game is much more thematic than TTR.  The event cards were cool, but it's weird how there are 3 per player at the beginning, then only one new card each turn.  There was a mad frenzy at the start, but then they had minimal impact.  I liked the income track, particularly how income peaks in the middle and then decreases.

Overall, I liked the game.  I enjoyed the route building, shipping, variable player powers (they were minor), and bonus points for select routes.  My only minor complaint was the shortage of event cards.  I'm looking forward to trying more rail games.

RATING: 7
A very interesting train game in which players are either elbowing each other for the lucrative but congested routes or else trying to find a clever exploit in the relatively ignored sections of the map.

It's also a very interesting economic game in that players have to balance spending to get first crack at the route they want while not getting too far into debt.

RATING: 5.5
In the beginning of the game there was a lot of tension. There were many interesting choices to be made with potentially lots of conflicting interests. But as the game progressed and everyone established his railway system, the game became really exceedingly static around mid-game, when everyone just delivered, delivered and once again: delivered! No one wanted to found a city or try anything else. One of the reasons it became this static is that drawing only one card after each round isn't nearly enough. In the beginning everyone bid on the starting player in order to be the first one to get access to the best connections or the best cards. As mentioned above later on there was no dire need to further expand the railway system and there were no cards or just one that just wasn't worthwhile. 
Not that my memory of Age of steam is very vivid, but from what I recall I think this one is better by a landslide.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 5
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

[02-15-09:Evaluation is based on both base game and Rails of Europe.]  After 3 plays: I love this game.  I've held off rating this so far because I'm waiting to play it more to see where the rating will settle.  It's a big, beefy box, with very tactile components.  I love the scale of the maps--the base game is huge and expansive and the Europe map still feels appropriately so.  Each game develops a narrative arc as you fight over territory, plot your own particular strategy, and determine the strengths of your empire--all while navigating transitions from broke start-up to burgeoning business to bloated and increasingly inefficient rail company.  I get a real sense of expansion and accomplishment from the game.  

I can see how Age of Steam would be more economically brutal, more cutthroat, more mathy, but at this point I honestly don't care, as this game strikes a great balance between playability and strategy that is very important for my gaming partners.

RATING: 9
This is very nearly a perfectly designed game.  You can teach the rules in 5 minutes to a complete gaming noobie.  I love that you just take one action at a time, because the downtime is almost nothing.  There is no need to ever reference the rules, yet there is a load of strategy involved.

I think that's the true measure of a great game for me, if its learned easily and you can literally use the rulebook as kindling after one read, and still have a deep experience with a solid amount of strategy.  That's gaming at it's best.  

Only dings on the base set is that some major lines don't come out which does not allow you to play the long game, and that the NE corridor is very very powerful.  Other maps fix both of these minor things too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played at the Eagle Games booth at GenCon. Really slick game that I can't wait to be released. We've played a lot of Age of Steam over the last few years, and while the core of the game is similar, these two games are different enough that they both can be enjoyed in their own right. The board is _huge_ and very spread out. Due to the city placement and they way resoursces work in the early game it's pretty obvious that you're supposed to start nearer to the east coast, and then expand to the west. Only three red cities makes them very valuable goods to ship, and competition for short connections was fierce at times. Differs from AoS in that bidding is for start player, and play then follows in clockwise order. Also, their are no role selections to be made, you simply take an action from a list of choices (play track, ship goods, urbanize, upgrade train, etc.) and take three actions per turn. Stocks still grant $5, and can be taken at any time, and after you take income (indicated on the scoring track) you pay 1 for each stock issued. Their are also special victory point cards delt out at the start of the game, for things like; issuing the least shares, having the longest consecutive line, having the largest locomotive, and so forth. There is also a special deck of action cards that alow for special point bonuses, extra track building, and so forth, but we didn't use these for the demo. Different, but similar, and I had a blast playing.

RATING: 5
An interesting eurogame that forces you into thinking like a capitalist! I'm not inspired by train-themed games, but this one flows well.

My only real complain is that player competition is on the bad side of player interaction - compete with one player, and the others get ahead. The combination of an uninspiring theme and "bad" interaction mechanics means that this game is really not for me.

RATING: 8
I came to Railroad Tycoon after having played Age of Steam over 30 times. I had some trepidation because of the warnings that other Age of Steam fans had given me about the significant inferiority of Railroad Tycoon. As a result, I was pleasantly surprised by Railroad Tycoon. I didn't like it quite as much as Age of Steam, but when it comes to Age of Steam, Railroad Tycoon, and now Steam, they're really all so much more similar than I think most people admit. I have a feeling most people just prefer the game among this trio that they happened to play first since it's most familiar, at least that's the case with me. I enjoy playing the other offshoots since they're basically just like variants and I don't see any reason to pick a single favorite that you need to stick with to the exclusion of the others. The essence of all three is the same and I'll happily play any of them just about any time.

RATING: 7

The game isn't as heavy as a 18XX title, not even close. It involves more pick-and-deliver and route building (including upgrading trains), and no shares buying and selling.

Overall, a good game in an enormous box.

RATING: 9
Definitely remains my top game. I love everything about the gameplay of this game.

I will admit it has many production flaws...overproduced plastic, the board warps, the hugeness of it yet the silly score track and lack of areas to put cards/stuff. the round markers on the board divide kills me. the colors.

And yet! None of those things, though they seem numerous, lessen my enjoyment of actually playing the game. I think I am obsessed a little with pick up and deliver mechanics and I can't explain why. All I know is I love building my little system, I love that everything is done with an eye on the competition, I love that I can try new things each time as the mix of cubes and cards opens up so many options at the start and even throughout the game you can try new directions or discover a lucrative delivery you really had no idea about. Will always want to play.

Knocking this down a notch only because Rails of Europe fixes the little tiny issues enough to make the original game...well, almost unnecessary!

RATING: 9.5
Very good rail game. Yes, it can be described as Age of Steam lite. However, I look at it as a different game with the same theme. RRT is definiterly less cut-throat. The economics are much milder and bankruptcy is no where in sight after the first few turns. The Operation and Tycoon cards add more theme to the game at the cost of a little more randomness. Allowing equal 'roles' and action opportunities adds more to the theme and removes the abstract feel of Age of Steam. RRT is a great game, suitable for family AND gamers.

UPDATE - After more plays I am more and more impressed with this game. Yes, there is a bias to the Northeast. However, there is a depth in this game and the theme runs so nicely with the gameplay that its a great game. As such I've upped my rating from the original 8.5 to 9.5.

RATING: 8
18XX unfortunately proved to be too heavy for the group, so this is our train game for now. It really takes up a huge amount of table space for a non-wargame.

Once the game is set up, it is easy to become a victim of information overload. You try to process the position of cubes and where they can be delivered, estimating where different possible networks would peak (at 3-deliveries? 4? 5? 6?), their cost, possible competition and bottlenecks, and it becomes quite a lot to take in...

Has become a popular game, and at some point I may look at getting the eastern US map.

RATING: 4
RAILROAD TYCOON DEVELOPMENT: A Play in One Act

FRED: Let's take Age of Steam, but strip out all of the interesting parts and package it under a different name!

MARTIN: Er ... what did you want to take out, exactly?

FRED: The auctions.

MARTIN:  The ... the auctions?  But--

FRED: Well, specifically the turn order relating to the auction.  It seems like we could just make it so that whoever bids highest goes first, and then everyone takes their turn clockwise from that point.

MARTIN: Doesn't that defeat the pur--

FRED: But you know what?  When we played Age of Steam, we got frustrated that we had to wait until it was our turn to increase our locomotive.  Why not just set it so that people can buy a stronger locomotive whenever they have the money, even if it's not their turn?

MARTIN: What?

FRED: Plus, the board is too small!  We want to produce a board the size of Wyoming, and make sure that there's so much space on the board that nineteen people could play at the same time, and never interfere with each other!

MARTIN:  That's ... oh my god....

FRED: And you know, Age of Steam needs CARDS, don't you think?  We could have a deck of cards where people could earn points for special goals, that no one else knows about, preventing anyone from knowingly keeping you from those goals?

MARTIN: The whole point of Age of Steam is to limit the amount of unknown information!

FRED: We know!  It sucks, doesn't it?  We want to make Age of Steam, just without any tension whatsoever!

MARTIN: ...

1 play: 5 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fun, accessible and beautiful. I like the balance of luck and skill. Production values are very good. It's neat that the game includes a larger map for 4-6 players (eastern United States) and a smaller map for 2-3 players (Mexico); this makes the game hit the table more often. The 2nd printing of RoTW also includes cards for the Mexico map, as well as matching and corrected cube and city colours and finally a very good plastic insert. Make sure you get the 2nd printing. I've yet to experience that the game overstays its welcome, so playing time is more or less just right. Make sure to get either Railways of Europe or Railways of England & Wales expansions. I'm not a fan of the large map included with Railways of the World. The two aforementioned expansions makes this game a 9. Highly recommended!

RATING: N/A
Railways of the World almost certainly represents the medium-weight railroad game at its best, being at the end of the process of evolutionary curve of development that began already before Age of Steam. It can be considered as the new-and-improved Railroad Tycoon Mark II, and effectively renders the original Railroad Tycoon game obsolete. It's already in the BGG Top 100, and will certainly rise above Railroad Tycoon in the rankings when it gets the same number of ratings, since its average rating is considerably higher.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is the new edition of Railroad Tycoon.

Railways of the World is one of my favorite games. In my eyes, it hits an almost perfect ratio of complexity to fun. There are plenty of opportunities for interesting decision making, multiple strategies to pursue and just enough tension to make it exciting. It also has gorgeous components that do an outstanding job of bringing the game to life. Win or lose, it's enjoyable just to watch your rail empire grow and spread across the continent. It's also neat to watch the empty city markers start to spring up around the board as the game wraps up. It gives the game an epic feel, as if you just played through a hundred years of history. There's nothing quite like it.

I'm also impressed with the milage the designers got out of such a simple rule set. It literally takes about 10 minutes to cover the basics, yet the mechanics feel so much deeper and complex than their simple framework. I also love the fact that everyone starts with zero money. From turn one, you're in debt. That alone makes most people approach the economics of this game from a bit outside their comfort zone.

It's a brilliant game and will be a fixture in my Top 10 for a long time to come.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Interesting game,  but a bit long.  The cube shipping for income/VP is a neat mechanic,  but feels slow-paced,  especially if you have lots of deliveries left to do and no one blocking you.  The blocking-style interaction that is there is pretty brutal and unforgiving (to the one trying to do the blocking),  and often feels a bit arbitrary - it's often difficult to block just for the sake of blocking (ie, not much blocking usually happens)

A couple more games - once on the new (Rails of Europe) map,  once again on the old one.   Definitely some pacing issues - the costs simply don't scale.   You're completely without money initially,  but by the end,  you can find yourself flush with cash and nothing reasonable to spend it (Western link is very rarely a reasonable proposition.).  That's probably a bit of player inefficiency,  and the fact that the auctions are only of mild importance - since it's "around from the winner" I don't think I ever bothered to spend much on the auction other than to bid up cards to (my perceived) value.  More of a "bid till people downstream from you pass and hope not to have to pay".

Mechanics feel solid,  the story arc just feels like it could be compressed into half the time.

Cards provide some goals and special abilities,  but feel a bit arbitrarily unbalanced.

USA map is (unnecesarily) big - "wide open" rather than tightly balanced is the feel that's given here.

RATING: N/A
Such a great train network, pick up and deliver. I love the open maps to lay track however you wish by paying different costs depending on terrain. It's a Wallace game so that means debt. What a great economic factor to allow debt leverage as far as you dare--brilliant. The trade between cities is so smartly simple. Upgrade your engine to complete longer linked deliveries. Draft auction objective cards for rewards or advantage ties in so well with history. Intuitive, easy to teach and then its a race to lay track. Turns are fast (mostly), eagerly await your next big move and more often than not, someone else takes it. Argh! But, there is always another move, and no one wins everything.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I purchased RRT because I love Age of Steam and was impressed by what I thought was a grand redesign and super-sizing of the original system.

Within a few months, however, the flaws of RRT became apparent.  The board is too big, which means that a player can be left all alone - resulting in a win for that player more often than not.  In addition, the randomness in RRT is over the top (major lines and service bounties popping into existence, etc.), the building chokepoints on the gameboard are endlessly frustrating, and the fact that the 3-link delivery bonus and the 4-level engine bonus are only one action apart severely unbalances the game in the favor of the early leader.  These glaring problems meant that RRT soon became a game that I would only play with people who found Age of Steam too unforgiving or too competitive.

But now I think I'm at the point where I won't play RRT at all.  If I had never played Age of Steam, I would continue to find RRT a moderately entertaining game. Compared to AoS, however, the flaws in RRT are numerous and severe enough that I just can't take the game seriously.

Edit:  The new edition (Railways of the World) fixes many of these issues.  Nice to hear.

All in all, I enjoy the game and if these little details were fixed, I would give the game and 8-8.5

RATING: 9
{ P - 3 [4] [5] 6 }

[b]Railroad Tycoon[/b]
I haven't played Age of Steam so I get the impression I'm settling for second best with Railroad Tycoon. That withstanding, my major beef with Tycoon is not really mechanics-related but rather lies with the blatant east coast dominance of the U.S. board and how nearly every facet of the game steers players in this direction. East coast cities are closely nested so the cost of building railroads over the course of the game is substantially cheaper. Their supply of goods is much deeper than the West and can easily fuel at least two players for the duration of the game. (Depletion of goods due to excess competition here isn't really detrimental either as this condition triggers the end-game, so the East can easily control the pace of the game.) These elements then bleed into the game's role cards because it's much more logical to build East to achieve most links, most contiguous links, fewest stock, most money, or first to L6 train. Ditto for many of the railroad cards, especially the major lines; the couple that spawn for the East are always of interest while something like New Orleans-Minneapolis can appear turn one and have no takers the entire game. The imbalance extends into the southwest area of the board and its automatic ghost town cities because they all require Perfect Engineering to access and even then are rarely worth the effort. Western Link suffers from the same problem, though there is a significant payoff associated if you manage to dedicate your entire strategy to forging one and succeed before too many cities become destitute and the game ends.

[b]Railways of England and Wales[/b]
Great expansion - somebody decided to fix nearly everything that was wrong with original Tycoon. Major lines no longer clog the deck of special cards and randomly reward players in that area when flipped because they are now objectives that begin on the board from turn one. Cities are much more reasonably spaced (no desolate breadbasket territory like Tycoon) and do not show preferential treatment to a single area of the board. More comments after future plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've played this game once now and once with a different map and it was a lot of fun. I really enjoy pickup and deliver games and games where you're building out your so-called "empire". There's something cool about being able to sit back see what you've accomplished on the table at the end of the game.

The game itself is quite simplistic with its rule set, which is great because it allows you to focus on strategy. It's also interesting how tight things get as the game progresses and you start to get on top of one another and agonize about whether it's better to spend money to make your own track or utilize someone else's at the cost of having to pay them and potentially putting them in a better position. It's also very tight because everyone starts with nothing and you're immediately forced to issues shares in your railroad... shares that you can never pay off, and ones that you have to pay out dividends to every round and count against you at the end of the game. The game ends up being a balancing act between making the most of the opportunities that are out on the board but not gambling too much and overextending yourself. The player that can walk this tightrope most effectively is the winner and the games that I have played so far have been very close.

The main downfall of the game is the sheer amount of space that it takes up. It's HUGE! And the base game board begs to be played with 4+ people. Because of this, it has sit on my shelf for quite some time. Having now played the North America map, I'm very much interested in acquiring it because I think that it'll allow me to play it more frequently due to the fact that it can accommodate fewer players well.

RATING: 8
A medium weight train game with quite a few paths to earning VPs and therefore victory.

The issuing of bonds is the most innovative part of this game's mechanic. One gets to decide how fast your initial buildup will be, but that produces a drag on your later income and eventual VP total. The event cards, railway baron cards, and the initial placement of goods cubes all add to the replayability of the game.

The game components seem overproduced though. This is especially with the empty city markers. Perhaps the cost of the game could be reduced if these were simplified.

RATING: 7
So far, only played with [thing=72267][/thing]...
This game and [thing=19995][/thing] have a fair amount in common.  Unlike Canal Mania, your connections are not driven/constrained by contract cards: You can build links anyplace where the delivery opportunities are ripe, or wherever you bloody well wish.  The loan system is a gentler sum of the schemes used in [thing=28720][/thing] and [thing=39683][/thing]:  You can't pay back "loans", they permanently detract from your income, and they reduce your score at the end of the game.
